⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فالصواب إلخ) أي الصواب أن لا يجعل الحكم نفسه ولا المركب منه، ومن غيره قسماً من العلم وأما إطلاق التصديق على التصور المقارن للحكم حتى ينقسم العلم إلى تصور ساذج، وإلى التصديق أي تصور معه حكم كما يتبادر من عبارة متن الكتاب فجائز، لكن يخالف وصف التصديق بالبداهة والظنية وغيرها فإنها أوصاف للحكم لا للتصور المقارن له، إلا أن يتسامح فيوصف ذلك التصور بوصف عارض له، وإنه تعسف . قوله : ( إلى تصور ساذج إلخ) والمقصود من التقسيم ظهور ذلك العارض المنفرد من معروضه بكاسب مخصوص وقد جعل بعضهم لفظ العلم مشتركاً بين المعروض، وذلك العارض، وقسم العلم إليهما فكأنه قيل : ما يطلق عليه لفظ العلم، إما تصور وإما حكم، وهو التصديق وتكلف آخرون بجعل الاشتراك معنوياً فقالوا : كان الأوائل قسموا المعاني الذهنية إلى نفس الإدراك، وإلى ما يلحقه وقسموا ما يلحقه إلى ما يجعله محتملاً للصدق والكذب، وإلى ما لا يجعله كذلك كالهيئات اللاحقة به في الأمر والنهي، والاستفهام والتمني وغير ذلك، وسموا المشترك بين القسمين الأولين علماً هذا كله على أن الحكم فعل، والصواب خلافه كذا نقل عنه . قوله: (كما وقع إلخ) وفي بعض النسخ كما ورد أي تقسيماً مماثلاً لما ورد في الكتب قوله : (كما توهمه العبارات إلخ قال الشارح في حواشي المطالع : لا عبرة بإيهام تلك العبارات فإن أهل اللغة لا يفرقون بين القبول والفعل، ويسمون القابل اسم فاعل والمقبول اسم مفعول وفيه نظر، إذ ليس الكلام في لفظ الفعل، والانفعال بل في مثل الإسناد، والإيقاع، ولا شك أن أهل اللغة وضعوها بإزاء الفعل فلا يجوز استعمالها بطريق الحقيقة في الكيف، والانفعال إلا مجازاً وهذا كما أنهم وضعوا بإزاء الفعل نحو الكسر وبإزاء الانفعال نحو الانكسار، فلا تقريب لما ذكره نعم لو استدل على فعلية الحكم بأن أهل اللغة يطلقون عليه الفعل وعلى الحاكم الفاعل، وعلى المحكوم به المفعول به لكان فيما ذكره تقريب ظاهر . قوله : ( فالصواب أن يقسم العلم إلخ) فعلى هذا يلزم توقف التصديق على خمسة أشياء. قوله : ( كما ورد في بعض الكتب المعتبرة قيل عليه قاسم العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين بعض الكتب المعتبرة هو أبو علي بن سينا، كما نقله في شرح المطالع، والحكم عنده إدراك لا فعل، فما ذكره صلح لا عن تراضي الخصمين والجواب أن مراد الشارح أن الصواب حينئذ أن يقسم مطلق العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين والشيخ إنما قسم إليهما العلم التصوري لا مطلق العلم، كما صرح به الشارح في حواشيه على ذلك الشرح، فإن أراد ببعض الكتب المعتبرة غير كتاب الشيخ فالأمر ظاهر وإن أراد كتابه فالضمير في ورد راجع إلى تقسيم العلم بالمعنى ولعارضه المسمى بالحكم والتصديق طريق خاص آخر، وأما جعل التصديق قسماً من العلم مع تركيبه من الحكم، وغيره فلا وجه له فعلاً كان الحكم، أو إدراكاً
(His saying: 'The correct view, etc.') i.e., the correct view is that one should not make the judgment itself or the composite of it and something else a part of knowledge. As for applying 'tasdiq' to the conception accompanying judgment so that knowledge is divided into bare conception and into tasdiq—i.e., a conception with a judgment—as understood from the phrasing of the text of the book, it is permissible. However, it contradicts the description of tasdiq as self-evident, probable, etc., for these are descriptions of the judgment, not of the conception accompanying it, unless one is lenient and describes that conception by an accident accruing to it, and that is forced. His saying: (into bare conception, etc.) And the purpose of the division is the manifestation of that isolated accident from its subject by a specific acquisitive means. Some have made the word 'ilm' common between the subject and that accident, and divided knowledge into the two: as if it were said, 'What is called knowledge is either conception or judgment, which is tasdiq.' Others, by making the commonality conceptual, said: The early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and into what attaches to it, and they divided what attaches to it into that which makes it capable of truth and falsehood, and into that which does not make it so, such as the forms that attach to it in command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, etc. They called the common between the first two divisions 'ilm.' All this is on the view that judgment is an act, but the correct view is contrary to this, as it is transmitted from him. His saying: (as occurred, etc.) And in some copies 'as was reported,' i.e., a division similar to what was reported in the books. His saying: (as the expressions suggest, etc.) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Matali': 'No attention is paid to the suggestion of those expressions, for the people of language do not distinguish between acceptance and act; they call the acceptor a subject (ism fa'il) and the accepted an object (ism maf'ul).' There is a consideration, for the discussion is not about the word 'act' and 'passivity,' but about such things as predication, effectuation, etc. There is no doubt that the people of language have placed these expressions for the act, so it is not permissible to use them in a real sense for the quality and passivity except metaphorically. This is like how they placed for the act such as 'breaking' and for passivity such as 'being broken.' So there is no approximation in what he mentioned. Yes, if one argued for the actuality of judgment by saying that the people of language apply 'act' to it, 'agent' to the one judging, and 'object acted upon' to the thing judged, then there would be a clear approximation in what he mentioned. His saying: (So the correct view is to divide knowledge, etc.) According to this, tasdiq would depend on five things. His saying: (as reported in some authoritative books) It was said against him: 'The one who divided knowledge into the two mentioned divisions from some authoritative books is Abu Ali ibn Sina, as transmitted in Sharh al-Matali', and judgment according to him is perception, not an act. So what he mentioned is a reconciliation, not from the mutual satisfaction of the two opponents.' The answer is that the commentator intended that the correct view then is to divide absolute knowledge into the two mentioned divisions, while the Shaykh only divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge, as the commentator clarified in his glosses on that commentary. If he intended by 'some authoritative books' other than the Shaykh's book, the matter is clear; if he intended his book, then the pronoun in 'occurred' refers to the division of knowledge in the sense. And for its accident called judgment and tasdiq, there is another specific path. As for making tasdiq a part of knowledge while it is composed of judgment and something else, it has no justification, whether judgment is an act or perception.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating that the correct view is not to make judgment (hukm) itself or a composite of judgment and conception a separate part of knowledge. The division into bare conception (tasawwur saadhij) and conception accompanied by judgment (tasdiq) is permissible, but there is a problem: descriptions like 'self-evident' or 'probable' apply to the judgment, not to the accompanying conception, unless one is lax. The passage then explains that the purpose of the division is to show that each type has its own acquisitive means. Some scholars made the term 'ilm' common between the subject (the conception) and the accident (the judgment), but others insisted on a conceptual commonality: the early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and its attachments, then subdivided attachments into those that allow truth/falsity and those that do not (e.g., imperative, interrogative forms). This was based on judgment being an act, but the correct view rejects that. Next, a linguistic argument is refuted: the commentator of al-Matali' notes that language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical reality; words like 'predication' (isnad) are placed for acts, not for qualities or passions. However, if one argues from language (calling judgment 'act' and the judge 'agent') it would be more plausible. Then the passage addresses an objection: the correct division leads to tasdiq depending on five things. It clarifies that Ibn Sina's division was for conceptual knowledge only, not absolute knowledge, so the objection is answered. Finally, it concludes that there is no justification for making tasdiq a separate part of knowledge, whether judgment is an act or a perception.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm)
عارض ('arid)
كاسب مخصوص (kāsib makhṣūṣ)
اشتراك (ishtirāk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ilm' has a univocal meaning across conception and judgment.
Correction: Scholars differ: some consider 'ilm' common, others see it as conceptually common, and the correct view differs.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of dividing knowledge into conception and assent according to the passage?
A) To distinguish between true and false beliefs
B) To show that each type has its own means of acquisition
C) To prove that judgment is an act
D) To reject the early scholars' views

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge serves to highlight that each type has its own means of acquisition, and scholars have debated the relationship between conception and judgment under the term 'ilm'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat 'knowing about something' (conception) as enough, or do you actively seek judgment (tasdiq) to confirm truth? Which one do you tend to neglect, and how does that affect your certainty?
?
The early scholars debated the nature of 'ilm. If the correct view is that judgment is not an act but a perception, how does that change your responsibility in verifying the truth of your beliefs? What will you do differently to ensure your judgments are genuine perceptions rather than mere acts?
?
Consider a core belief you hold about God or the unseen. Is it merely a conception (an idea you have heard) or a tasdiq that you have truly perceived as truth? If it falls short, what step can you take today to move from conception to assent?

Linguistic Argument and Resolution of Objection about Ibn Sina
The author discusses a linguistic argument and an objection by Ibn Sina, then resolves them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument about judgment as an act and how the objection regarding Ibn Sina's division is resolved.

Core Idea
Linguistic usage does not settle the metaphysical nature of judgment.

The author addresses a linguistic argument that since language calls judgment an 'act' and the judge an 'agent', judgment must be an act. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing language from reality. Then an objection is raised: the division of knowledge into conception and assent is attributed to Ibn Sina, but he only divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge. The answer clarifies that the correct division applies to absolute knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
This is the longest passage. Read it once to get the overall flow: linguistic argument, refutation, then objection and answer.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument and refutation
Identify the claim that language shows judgment is an act, and the commentator's response. Notice the distinction between grammatical forms and philosophical reality.
The commentator says language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical facts.
3
Examine the objection about Ibn Sina
The objection states that Ibn Sina divided knowledge into conception and assent, but he considered judgment a perception, not an act. Find the answer given.
The answer is that Ibn Sina divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating that the correct view is not to make judgment (hukm) itself or a composite of judgment and conception a separate part of knowledge. Then the passage addresses an objection: the correct division leads to tasdiq depending on five things. It clarifies that Ibn Sina's division was for conceptual knowledge only, not absolute knowledge, so the objection is answered. Finally, it concludes that there is no justification for making tasdiq a separate part of knowledge, whether judgment is an act or a perception.

Key Takeaway

?
The passage concludes that making tasdiq a part of knowledge has no justification, whether judgment is an act or perception. Does this unsettle your understanding of the relationship between knowledge, belief, and certitude? Are you willing to re-examine your own epistemology—how you claim to 'know' something—in light of this rigorous intellectual scrutiny?

The Essential Distinction Between Conception and Assent
Defines tasawwur and tasdiq as two types distinct in essence and by truth/falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways in which tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) are distinguished: by essence and by the concomitant of truth/falsity.

Core Idea
Conception and assent differ both in their very nature and in whether they can be true or false.

The author begins by establishing that the two fundamental types of knowledge, conception and assent, are not merely different by convention but are distinct in their essence. He then provides an intuitive example to make this clear, and finally adds a well-known rule-of-thumb distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic—they indicate the original text of the matn.
2
Understand the example
The passage gives an example: when you doubt a relation between two things, you still have conception of those things and the relation. Then when you judge, you have assent. Reflect on how these two states feel different.
3
Grasp the essential distinction
The author says the two types are distinct 'by essence' (bi al-dhat), meaning their very quiddity (mahiyya) is different. The example illustrates this intuitive difference.
This is not just a matter of definition but a real difference we can experience.
4
Learn the concomitant distinction
The second distinction is by a well-known concomitant: the possibility of truth and falsehood. Assent can be true or false, while conception cannot. This is a practical way to tell them apart.
This is a 'lazim' (necessary property) that helps identify each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وهما ) أي التصور والتصديق ( نوعان متمايزان بالذات ) أي بالماهية فإنك إذا تصورت نسبة أمر إلى آخر، وشككت فيها فقد علمت ذينك الأمرين، والنسبة بينهما قطعاً فلك في هذه الحالة نوع من العلم، ثم إذا زال عنك الشك، وحكمت بأحد طرفي النسبة فقد علمت تلك النسبة نوعاً آخر من العلم، ممتازاً عن الأول بحقيقته وجدانا ( وباعتبار اللازم المشهور وهو احتمال الصدق والكذب ) في التصديق ( وعدمه ) في التصور .
(And they two) i.e., conception and assent (are two types distinct in essence) i.e., in quiddity. For if you conceive a relation of one thing to another and doubt it, you have definitely known those two things and the relation between them, so in this state you have a type of knowledge. Then when the doubt leaves you and you judge one of the two sides of the relation, you have known that relation as another type of knowledge, distinguished from the first by its reality by direct intuition. (And by the consideration of the well-known concomitant, namely the possibility of truth and falsehood) in assent, (and its absence) in conception.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of knowledge—tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent)—as distinct in essence (bi al-dhat) and by a well-known concomitant. It first explains the essential distinction: if you merely conceive a relation between two things while doubting it, you have a type of knowledge (tasawwur of the terms and relation). Then, when doubt is removed and you judge one side of the relation, you have another type of knowledge (tasdiq), which is intuitively different. Thus they differ in their very quiddity. The second distinction is by the necessary concomitant of possibility of truth and falsehood: tasdiq admits truth/falsity, while tasawwur does not. This is a standard kalam distinction.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
بالذات (bi al-dhat)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
احتمال الصدق والكذب (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kidhb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the distinction between conception and assent is only by an external accident (like the accompanying judgment) rather than by essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are distinct 'by essence' (bi al-dhat), meaning their very natures differ, not merely an added property. The example shows they are different types of knowledge in themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one way tasawwur and tasdiq are distinguished?
A) By their subject matter
B) By the possibility of truth and falsehood
C) By being based on revelation
D) By being certain or probable

Key Takeaway
Conception and assent are fundamentally different types of knowledge: they differ in their very essence and in whether they can be evaluated as true or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, are you merely conceiving the words (tasawwur) or have you truly assented (tasdiq) to their meaning in a way that changes your actions? What evidence in your life would show the difference?
?
Think of a belief about Allah that you hold. Is your knowledge of it merely conceptual—you understand the words—or have you judged it to be true in your heart? How would you know the difference, and which do you want to strengthen?
?
The passage says assent can be false. Which belief do you hold most dear, and are you willing to test whether it is true or false using the criteria of sound reason and revelation? What would it cost you if you discovered it was only a conception you assumed was true?

Al-Qadi Abu Bakr's Definition of Necessary Knowledge and Its Defense
Al-Qadi Abu Bakr defines necessary knowledge and addresses two objections about its loss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi Abu Bakr's definition of necessary knowledge and how his response to the objections clarifies the scope of the definition.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge clings to the soul, not permanently, but beyond the creature's power to remove.

Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani defines necessary knowledge as knowledge that clings to the creature's self with no way to separate. Two objections are raised: that it can be lost through opposites like sleep, and that it can be absent before its cause. The response refines the definition by showing that 'no way to separate' means lack of voluntary power, not absolute permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow. Notice the definition, the two objections, and the response.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the definition and its examples
Find al-Qadi's definition of necessary knowledge: knowledge that clings to the self such that the creature has no way to separate from it. Note the examples given: knowledge of the permissibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things.
The definition uses the key term 'لا يجد إليه سبيلا'.
3
Understand the two objections
The first objection: necessary knowledge can be lost by its opposites like sleep and forgetfulness. The second: necessary knowledge may be absent before its cause, like before sense experience. Both challenge the 'clinging' claim.
These objections are introduced with 'أورد عليه' and 'أورد أيضاً'.
4
Analyze the response
The response clarifies that 'finds no way to separate' means the creature has no power to separate voluntarily. The objects' loss or absence is not within the creature's power, so they do not contradict the definition.
The response draws on Arabic usage of 'يجد إليه سبيلا' to imply power.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this nuance matters: necessary knowledge is not eternal, but it is not subject to voluntary removal. This preserves the intuitive understanding of necessary knowledge while accommodating observable loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالضروري قال القاضي أبو بكر في تفسيره هو العلم الذي يلزم نفس المخلوق لزوماً لا يجد المخلوق إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلا كالعلم بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات وأورد عليه جواز زواله أي زوال العلم الضروري بعد حصوله بأضداده كالنوم والغفلة وأورد أيضاً أنه قد يفقد العلم الضروري لعدم مقتضيه كما يفقد قبل الحس أي الإحساس والوجدان وسائر ما يتوقف عليه من التواتر والتجربة وتوجه العقل فلا يكون العلم الضروري لازماً لنفس المخلوق لا دائما ولا بعد حصوله ولا يرد على ورد عليه إذ عبارته مشعرة بالقدرة أي باعتبار مفهوم القدرة في التعريف منفية فإنك إذا قلت فلان يجد إلى كذا سبيلا يفهم منه أنه يقدر عليه وإذا قلت لا يجد إليه سبيلا فهم منه أنه لا يقدر عليه فمراد القاضي أن الانفكاك عن العلم الضروري ليس مقدوراً للمخلوق وما ذكرتم من زواله بأضداده وفقده قبل ما يقتضيه لا ينافي مراده إذ ليس شيء منهما انفكاكاً مقدوراً بل ليس بمقدور
The necessary [knowledge] – al-Qadi Abu Bakr said in his explanation: it is the knowledge that clings to the creature's self with a clinging such that the creature finds no way to separate from it, like knowledge of the permissibility of permissible things and the impossibility of impossible things. And it was objected to it: the possibility of its loss – meaning the loss of necessary knowledge after its occurrence – by its opposites like sleep and forgetfulness. And it was also objected: that necessary knowledge may be absent due to the absence of its cause, as it is absent before sense perception – i.e., sensation, intuition, and everything that depends on it of tawatur, experience, and the application of reason – so necessary knowledge is not necessary for the creature's self, neither perpetually nor after its occurrence. And it is not [valid] as an objection against what is objected to him, because his expression implies power – i.e., by considering the concept of power negated in the definition. For if you say: 'So-and-so finds a way to such-and-such,' it is understood that he has power over it; and if you say: 'He finds no way to it,' it is understood that he has no power over it. So al-Qadi's intent is that separation from necessary knowledge is not within the creature's power, and what you mentioned of its loss by opposites and its absence before its cause does not contradict his intent, for neither of them is a separation that is within power; rather it is not within power.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) according to al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. It begins by defining it as knowledge that necessarily adheres to the creature's self such that the creature cannot find any way to separate from it. Examples include knowledge of the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things. Two objections are then raised: first, that necessary knowledge can be lost after acquisition through opposites like sleep and forgetfulness; second, that it can be absent before its cause, such as before sense experience. Consequently, the objection claims, necessary knowledge is not perpetually necessary nor necessary after it occurs. The response defends the definition by pointing out that the phrasing 'no way to separate' implies lack of power, not absolute inseparability. According to Arabic usage, saying 'so-and-so finds no way to something' indicates he has no power over it. Therefore, al-Qadi meant that separation from necessary knowledge is beyond the creature's power. The two objections – loss by opposites and absence before the cause – are not instances of a separation that is within the creature's power, so they do not refute the definition.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
الانفكاك
القدرة
اللزوم
الأضداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge can never be lost under any circumstances.
Correction: The definition means that the creature cannot voluntarily separate from it, but it can be lost involuntarily (e.g., through sleep).

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the response to the objection that necessary knowledge can be lost by opposites?
A) The definition requires that necessary knowledge never be lost under any circumstances.
B) The definition's phrase 'finds no way to separate' refers to inability to separate by choice, not absolute permanence.
C) The objection is invalid because necessary knowledge is eternal.
D) The definition is only about knowledge of God, not human knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi Abu Bakr's definition of necessary knowledge centers on the creature's inability to voluntarily separate from it, not on absolute permanence.

Refining the Response: Two Interpretations of Inseparability
Refining the response to an objection about the definition of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refines the definition of necessary knowledge by distinguishing two senses of 'inseparability' and why this distinction matters for the validity of the objection.

Core Idea
The definition of necessary knowledge hinges on whether 'inseparability' means absolute permanence or inability to separate.

This passage delves deeper into the objection that separation contradicts the 'clinging' in the definition of necessary knowledge. The commentator offers two possible interpretations of what the original author meant by 'clinging', showing that the objection can be answered either by qualifying the term or by reinterpreting it. Understanding this refinement is crucial for grasping the nuanced logical interplay in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection restated
The objector insists that any separation, whether voluntary or involuntary, contradicts the 'clinging' in the definition, so the objection remains. Recognize that this challenges the earlier response.
The objector is not convinced by the initial answer.
2
Examine the two interpretations
The responder proposes two possible meanings of 'clinging': (1) absolute permanence qualified by the condition that separation is not within power, or (2) absolute inseparability, meaning the knowledge never separates as long as it exists.
The first interpretation allows for separation after existence; the second does not.
3
Analyze the commentator's critique of wording
The commentator suggests the proper phrasing should focus on the creature's inability to find a way to separate, not on perpetual clinging. This shifts the definition from positive attachment to negative impossibility.
The wording 'not finding separation' is more precise.
4
Reflect on the ordering of objections
The commentator notes that the text's order could be improved by mentioning the second objection (absence before cause) before the first (loss by opposites), as the first deals with loss after existence and the second with absence before existence.
This shows careful logical sequencing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت الانفكاك مقدوراً كان أو غير مقدور ينافي اللزوم المذكور في التعريف فالسؤال باق بحاله قلت لعله أراد باللزوم الثبوت مطلقاً ثم قيده بكون الانفكاك عنه غير مقدور أو أراد الانفكاك عنه مطلقاً لأنه قد انفك في بعض الأوقات فلا يرد أنه في وقت الفقدان ليس بعلم حادث فهو خارج عن المقسم قوله فلا يكون العلم الضروري لازماً إلخ الظاهر أن يقول فلا يكون العلم الضروري مما لا يجد المخلوق إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً لا دائماً ولا بعد حصوله لأن منشأ الاعتراض ليس أخذ اللزوم في التعريف بل عدم وجدان الانفكاك إلا أنه تسامح فوضع ما هو لازم عدم الوجدان مقامه اعتمادا على ظهور المقصود وفي تقديم قوله لا دائما ولا بعد حصوله إشارة إلى ما قلنا من أن اللائق تقديم قوله وإنه قد يفقد على قوله جواز زواله
For if you say: separation, whether within power or not, contradicts the clinging mentioned in the definition, so the question remains as it was. I say: perhaps he intended by 'clinging' absolute permanence, then conditioned it by the separation from it being not within power; or he intended separation from it absolutely, because it does separate in some times, so it is not an objection that at the time of absence it is not originated knowledge, for that falls outside the division. His saying: 'So necessary knowledge is not necessary, etc.' Apparently he should say: 'So necessary knowledge is not something from which the creature finds no way to separate, neither perpetually nor after its occurrence,' because the source of the objection is not the taking of 'clinging' in the definition, but rather the not finding separation; however, he made a concession and put that which is necessary for the not finding in its place, relying on the clarity of the intent. And in his precedence of saying 'neither perpetually nor after its occurrence' there is an indication to what we said: that it is appropriate to preface his saying 'And it may be absent' before his saying 'the possibility of its loss.'
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage provides a deeper analysis of the response to the objection. The objector may insist that any separation, whether voluntary or involuntary, contradicts the 'clinging' (luzūm) mentioned in the definition, so the objection still stands. The commentator offers two possible interpretations of what al-Qadi meant by luzūm: (1) He meant absolute permanence (thubūt muṭlaqan), but then qualified it by the condition that separation is not within power. (2) He meant absolute inseparability, but since necessary knowledge does separate in some cases (e.g., at times of absence before its cause), he was actually referring to the creature's inability to find a way to separate, not literal permanent attachment. The commentator suggests that the proper phrasing should have been that necessary knowledge is something from which the creature finds no way to separate, not necessarily that it is always present. The objection's basis is the lack of finding separation, not the presence of clinging. The author of the commentary (the sharh) also notes that the order of the objections in the text could be improved by mentioning the second objection (absence before cause) before the first (loss by opposites), as the first is about loss after occurrence and the second about absence before occurrence.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الانفكاك
الثبوت المطلق
القسمة
المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection is definitively refuted by the first response and no further analysis is needed.
Correction: This passage shows that the original response requires refinement and that the meaning of 'clinging' is debated, revealing the depth of the theological argument.

Checkpoint
What two possible meanings does the commentator suggest for 'luzūm' (clinging) in the definition?

B) Absolute permanence qualified by inability to separate, and absolute inseparability.
C) Power-relative inseparability and factual inseparability.
D) Logical necessity and causal necessity.

Key Takeaway
The definition of necessary knowledge is refined by distinguishing between absolute permanence and actual inability to separate, showing the depth of rational debate in Islamic theology.

Definition of Necessary Knowledge and Its Implication of Inability
Al-Jurjani defines necessary knowledge and defends its definition against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of necessary knowledge as that which the creature cannot avoid by its own power, and how this definition is defended against the objection that necessary knowledge can be lost.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is about inability to avoid, not about permanence.

In this passage, al-Jurjani defines necessary knowledge and addresses a critical objection. The definition hinges on the phrase 'la yajidu ilayhi sabilan' (does not find a way to it), which implies that the creature has no power to avoid such knowledge. However, one might object that necessary knowledge can be lost or cease, contradicting the idea of necessity. Al-Jurjani and the commentator clarify that the necessity in the definition refers only to the inability to avoid, not to absolute permanence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division and definition
Read the opening lines where occurring knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired, and necessary knowledge is defined. Note the key expression 'la yajidu ilayhi sabilan' and its implication of inability.
The definition is given indirectly through the negation of finding a way.
2
Examine the objection
The objection states that necessary knowledge cannot be permanent because it may be removed by opposites or absent before its cause, so how can it be called necessary? Identify this objection in the passage.
The objection challenges the term 'luzum' (necessity).
3
Analyze the answer
The answer is that the definition does not require absolute permanence; rather, it only negates the creature's power to avoid it. The definition implicitly includes the concept of power (qudra) negated.
The commentator clarifies that separation from necessary knowledge is not within the creature's power.
4
Delve into the two interpretations of 'luzum'
The commentator discusses two ways to interpret 'luzum' in the definition: as absolute existence or as impossibility of avoidable separation. The second interpretation is preferred because it aligns with the negation of power.
This part is more advanced but crucial for a precise understanding.
5
Reflect on the lesson
The key takeaway is that necessary knowledge is not necessarily eternal; it is simply knowledge that the creature cannot voluntarily avoid. Reflect on how this definition avoids the objection.
Consider how this definition fits into the broader classification of knowledge.

الإحساس ( والوجدان ) وسائر ما يتوقف عليه من التواتر ،والتجربة، وتوجه العقل فلا يكون العلم الضروري لازماً لنفس المخلوق، لا دائما ولا بعد حصوله، ( ولا يرد) على ورد عليه ( إذ عبارته مشعرة بالقدرة ) أي باعتبار مفهوم القدرة في التعريف منفية فإنك إذا قلت فلان يجد إلى كذا سبيلا ، يفهم منه أنه يقدر عليه، وإذا قلت : لا يجد إليه سبيلا فهم منه أنه لا يقدر عليه، فمراد القاضي أن الانفكاك عن العلم الضروري ليس مقدوراً للمخلوق وما ذكرتم من زواله بأضداده وفقده قبل ما يقتضيه لا ينافي مراده، إذ ليس شيء منهما انفكاكاً مقدوراً ، بل ليس بمقدور فإن قلت : الانفكاك مقدوراً كان أو غير مقدور ينافي اللزوم المذكور في التعريف، فالسؤال باق بحاله، قلت : لعله أراد باللزوم الثبوت مطلقاً ، ثم قيده بكون الانفكاك عنه غير مقدور، أو أراد الانفكاك عنه مطلقاً، لأنه قد انفك في بعض الأوقات، فلا يرد أنه في وقت الفقدان ليس بعلم حادث، فهو خارج عن المقسم. قوله : ( فلا يكون العلم الضروري لازماً إلخ الظاهر أن يقول : فلا يكون العلم الضروري مما لا يجد المخلوق إلي الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً، لا دائماً ولا بعد حصوله، لأن منشأ الاعتراض ليس أخذ اللزوم في التعريف، بل عدم وجدان الانفكاك إلا أنه تسامح فوضع ما هو لازم عدم الوجدان مقامه، اعتمادا على ظهور المقصود، وفي تقديم قوله لا دائما ولا بعد حصوله إشارة إلى ما قلنا من أن اللائق تقديم قوله وإنه قد يفقد على قوله جواز زواله . قوله : (فهم منه أنه لا يقدر عليه مع عدم حصوله وها هنا كذلك لأن الانفكاك غير حاصل في وقت حصول العلم الضروري . قوله : ( فإن قلت إلخ) يعني النقض المذكور وإن اندفع بالنظر إلى قوله لا يجد إلخ لكنه باق بالنظر إلى قوله : يلزم ثم لا يخفى عليك أن تقرير الإيراد بالنظر إلى قوله لا يجد إلى الانفكاك سبيلاً، يستلزم استدراك لفظ الجواز في قوله جواز زواله، كما يشير إليه قول الشارح، وما ذكرتم من زواله بالأضداد فالأولى أن تقرير الإيراد بالنظر إلى قيد اللزوم المذكور في التعريف، فإن اللزوم ينافي جواز الزوال، وإنه قد يفقد لفقدان المقتضي، وتقرير الجواب بأن القدرة معتبرة في التعريف، فالمراد باللزوم امتناع الانفكاك المقدور وحينئذ لا يكون الإيراد واحداً فتدبر.
قوله : (فهم منه أنه لا يقدر عليه فمراد القاضي إلخ فيه بحث لأنا سلمنا أن هذا الكلام يفيد في العرف نفي القدرة، لكن مع عدم الحصول، فإذا قيل : فلان لا يجد سبيلاً إلى كذا يفهم منه أنه غير حاصل له وغير قادر على تحصيله فتجويز صدق التعريف عند حصول أصل الانفكاك مع نفي القدرة إخراج له عن المتبادر . قوله: (قلت لعله أراد باللزوم الثبوت مطلقاً الحق الصريح هو الجواب الثاني، لأن المفعول المطلق مبين للمراد من عامله وأما إرادة الثبوت المطلق من اللزوم، فمن قبيل المجاز الخفي قرينته والأولى أن يجتنب في التعريف عن مثله .
به امتناع الانفكاك المقدور فيكون آخر كلامه تفسيراً لأوله
Third Observatory - Second Intention: Occurring knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired. Its definition: what? Sensation (and inner sense) and all that depends on it from recurrent reports, experience, and direction of the mind. So necessary knowledge is not necessary for the creature itself, neither always nor after its occurrence. (And it is not refuted) by what is refuted (because its expression indicates power) i.e., considering the concept of power in the definition is negated. For if you say: So-and-so finds a way to something, it is understood that he is able to do it. And if you say: He does not find a way to it, it is understood that he is not able to do it. So al-Qadi's intent is that separation from necessary knowledge is not within the creature's power. And what you mentioned of its being removed by its opposites and its absence before what necessitates it does not contradict his intent, because neither of them is a separation that is within power; rather it is not within power. For if you say: Separation, whether within power or not, contradicts the necessity mentioned in the definition, so the question remains as it was. I say: Perhaps he intended by necessity absolute existence, then he restricted it by the separation from it not being within power, or he intended separation from it absolutely, because it has separated in some times, so it is not objected that at the time of absence it is not occurring knowledge, so it is outside the division. His saying: (So necessary knowledge is not necessary etc.) The apparent is that he should say: So necessary knowledge is not something the creature finds no way to avoid, neither always nor after its occurrence, because the source of the objection is not taking necessity in the definition, but rather the non-finding of avoidance. Except that he was lenient and placed what is necessary for non-finding in its place, relying on the clarity of the intent. And in his advancing the saying 'neither always nor after its occurrence' there is an indication to what we said: that it is appropriate to advance his saying 'and that it may be absent' over his saying 'possibility of its removal'. His saying: (So it is understood that he is not able to do it while it is not obtained, and here it is likewise because avoidance is not obtained at the time of obtaining necessary knowledge. His saying: (For if you say etc.) means the mentioned refutation, although it is repelled with respect to his saying 'he does not find etc.', but it remains with respect to his saying 'it is necessary'. Then it is not hidden from you that presenting the objection with respect to his saying 'he does not find a way to avoidance' necessitates the recovery of the word 'possibility' in his saying 'possibility of its removal', as indicated by the commentator's saying. And what you mentioned of its removal by opposites, so it is better to present the objection with respect to the condition of necessity mentioned in the definition, for necessity is incompatible with the possibility of removal, and that it may be absent due to the absence of the cause. And the presentation of the answer is that power is considered in the definition, so what is meant by necessity is the impossibility of avoidable separation, and then the objection is not one. So contemplate. His saying: (So it is understood that he is not able to do it, so al-Qadi's intent etc.) There is discussion in it, for we concede that this speech in common usage indicates negation of power, but with non-attainment. So when it is said: So-and-so does not find a way to something, it is understood that it is not obtained by him and he is not able to obtain it. So allowing the truth of the definition at the occurrence of the original avoidance with negation of power takes it out of the obvious meaning. His saying: (I said: Perhaps he intended by necessity absolute existence.) The clear truth is the second answer, because the absolute object clarifies the intent of its verb. As for intending absolute existence from necessity, it is a kind of implicit metaphor whose indication is [unclear] and it is better to avoid the like in definitions. By it, the impossibility of avoidable separation, so the last part of his speech is an interpretation of the first.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines necessary knowledge and addresses an objection. It begins by stating the division of occurring knowledge into necessary and acquired. It then gives the definition of necessary knowledge as that which the creature cannot find a way to avoid, as indicated by the expression 'he does not find a way to it' which implies inability. An objection is raised: if knowledge is necessary (lazim), it cannot be lost, yet it can be lost by opposites or before its cause. The answer is that the definition implicitly negates power (qudra); the necessity intended is not absolute but rather the inability to avoid by one's own power. The commentator then discusses two possible interpretations of 'luzum' in the definition: one as absolute existence, and another as the impossibility of avoidable separation. The passage concludes that the second interpretation is clearer.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
اللزوم
القدرة
الانفكاك
المخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessary knowledge must be permanent and cannot be lost.
Correction: The definition only requires that the creature cannot avoid it by its own will; loss by external causes or absence before its cause does not contradict necessity.

Checkpoint
What does the expression 'لا يجد إليه سبيلاً' (does not find a way to it) indicate according to the passage?
A) The knowledge is impossible to have
B) The creature is unable to avoid the knowledge
C) The knowledge is eternal
D) The creature has power over the knowledge

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge is defined by the creature's inability to avoid it, not by its permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What necessary knowledge do you possess that you cannot avoid, yet you sometimes act as if it were optional? How does your inability to avoid that truth demand a change in your daily life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge of Allah's existence, do you treat it as something you can push aside when convenient? What would it mean for you to fully submit to a knowledge you cannot escape?
?
The definition of necessary knowledge centers on inability to avoid. Is there a truth about your own soul that you have been trying to avoid? What would it cost you to stop evading it and let it transform you?

What Is Kalam?

The Objection That Theoretical Knowledge Becomes Necessary
Objection and response regarding theoretical knowledge and necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theoretical knowledge is not considered necessary despite being unavoidable after its occurrence, and how the definition of necessary knowledge accounts for the power to avoid before acquisition.

Core Idea
Avoidability before acquisition distinguishes theoretical from necessary knowledge.

Al-Jurjani now addresses a subtle objection: if theoretical knowledge becomes unavoidable once acquired, then it seems to fit the definition of necessary knowledge. The response hinges on the timing of power—whether one could have avoided the knowledge before it was acquired. This lesson unpacks the objection and the precise definition of necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify that the objection claims theoretical knowledge after occurrence is unavoidable, thus necessary.
2
Understand the response about absolute vs. conditional avoidance
The response says the definition requires inability to avoid absolutely (both before and after), but theoretical can be avoided before.
3
Examine the summarized definition
Notice the summarized definition: necessary knowledge is that whose acquisition is not within the creature's power.
4
Consider the entailment discussion
The text discusses whether if acquisition is not within power, avoidance is also not within power, and how power is defined as ability for two opposites.
This part is cut off in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فكذا النظري بعد حصوله أي هو أيضاً غير مقدور انفكاكه، إذ لا قدرة للمخلوق على الانفكاك عنه بعد حصوله، فيدخل في حد الضروري، والفاء في قوله فكذا، للإشعار بترتب هذا السؤال على الجواب عن السؤال الأول : قلنا : لا يلزم من عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عن النظري بعد حصوله عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عنه مطلقاً والمذكور في التعريف هو عدم القدرة على الانفكاك مطلقاً، وذلك إنما يوجد في الضروري، وأما النظري فمقدور انفكاكه قبل حصوله، بأن يترك النظر فيه ونقول نحن في تلخيص تعريف القاضي هو ما لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً للمخلوق، فإذا لم يكن تحصيله
قوله : (ثم قيده إلخ) بأن يكون قوله لا يجد إلخ صفة للزوماً فيكون المفعول المطلق للنوع كما في ضربت ضرباً شديداً.
قوله: (فيكون آخر كلامه إلخ بأن يكون قوله : لا يجد جملة لا محل لها من الإعراب مفسرةً لقوله : يلزم والفرق بين الجوابين أن اللزوم على الأول محمول على المعنى اللغوي، وعلى الثاني على المعنى الاصطلاحي .

قوله : ( ونقول : نحن إلخ لم يظهر لي وجه زيادة نحن في التاج التلخيص هو يداكردن، ففيه إشارة إلى أن التعريفين متحدان مفهوما لا فرق بينهما إلا باعتبار الخفاء والظهور، فلا يرد أنه لم لم يحمله على أنه تعريف برأسه كما هو الظاهر المتبادر ، وكون التعريفين متلازمين في الصدق، لا يقتضي كون أحدهما ملخص الآخر كالتعريف المختار والأحسن للعلم . قوله : هو ما لا يكون تحصيله إلخ أي العلم الحادث الذي لا يكون إلخ، فلا يرد العلم بالأمور الغير المتناهية كالأعداد والأشكال. قوله : (فإذا لم يكن تحصيله إلخ) وذلك لأنه لا معنى للقدرة إلا التمكن من الطرفين، فإذا كان التحصيل مقدوراً يكون تركه الذي هو التحصيل مقدوراً، فاندفع ما توهم من منع الملازمة
قوله : (هو ما لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً إلخ إلا أن قيد الحصول مراد ها هنا بقرينة جعل الضروري من أقسام العلم الحادث. قوله : ( فإذا لم يكن تحصيله مقدوراً لم يكن الانفكاك عنه مقدوراً) منع الملازمة بجوار توقف حصول شيء على أشياء بعضها مقدور كالإحساس دون بعض فيصدق أن تحصيله غير مقدور وإن كان تركه مقدوراً، قيل : ويمكن أن يكون السبب في عدول المصنف إلى ما ذكره من التعريف هذا وإن لم يذكره الشارح لا يقال من شرط القدرة صحة تعلقها بالضدين على
For if it is said: similarly, theoretical knowledge after its occurrence, i.e., it also is not within power to avoid, for the creature has no power to avoid it after its occurrence, so it enters the definition of necessary. And the 'fa' in his saying 'similarly' indicates the ordering of this question upon the answer to the first question. We said: It does not follow from the inability to avoid theoretical knowledge after its occurrence the inability to avoid it absolutely; and what is mentioned in the definition is the inability to avoid absolutely, and that is only found in the necessary. As for theoretical, its avoidance is within power before its occurrence, by leaving the reasoning about it. And we say, we, in summarizing al-Qadi's definition: it is that whose acquisition is not within the creature's power. So if its acquisition is not within power... His saying: (So the last part of his speech etc.) such that his saying 'he does not find' is a nominal sentence with no grammatical position, explanatory for his saying 'it is necessary'. So it is not objected that he did not take it as a definition by itself as is apparent and obvious. And the two definitions being co-extensive in truth does not require that one be a summary of the other, like the chosen and best definition for knowledge. His saying: (It is that whose acquisition is not within power etc.) i.e., the occurring knowledge that is not etc., so there is no objection about knowledge of infinite things like numbers and shapes. His saying: (So if its acquisition is not within power etc.) And that is because there is no meaning to power except the ability for both sides. So if acquisition is within power, its leaving, which is the acquisition, is within power. So what was imagined of denying the entailment is repelled. His saying: (It is that whose acquisition is not within power etc.) except that the condition of acquisition is intended here by the context of making the necessary a division of occurring knowledge. His saying: (So if its acquisition is not within power, then its avoidance is not within power.) The entailment is denied due to the possibility of a thing's acquisition depending on some things that are within power, like sensation, and some that are not, so it is true that its acquisition is not within power even if its leaving is within power. It was said: And it is possible that the reason for the author's turning to the definition he mentioned is this, even if the commentator did not mention it. It cannot be said that one of the conditions of power is its validity to attach to both opposites...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection: if necessary knowledge is defined as that which the creature cannot avoid, then after theoretical knowledge occurs, it also cannot be avoided, so it would be necessary. The response is that the inability is not absolute; before its acquisition, one can avoid it by not engaging in reasoning. The definition requires that there be no power to avoid it absolutely (both before and after), which is only true of necessary knowledge. The commentator then presents a summarized definition: necessary knowledge is that whose acquisition is not within the creature's power. He then discusses an objection to the entailment: if acquisition is not within power, then avoidance (by leaving) is also not within power, but this is denied because power is defined as the ability to choose both opposites; if acquisition is not within power, then its opposite (leaving) might still be within power if it depends on voluntary actions like sensation. But this is resolved because sensation is a voluntary act, so if acquisition depends on it, then acquisition is indirectly voluntary? Actually the text ends mid-argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if knowledge after its occurrence is unavoidable, it must be necessary.
Correction: The definition of necessary knowledge requires absolute inability to avoid, including before acquisition. Theoretical knowledge can be avoided before acquisition by refraining from reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does theoretical knowledge not qualify as necessary knowledge even though it cannot be avoided after its occurrence?
A) Because theoretical knowledge is not occurring
B) Because it can be avoided before it occurs by refraining from reasoning
C) Because theoretical knowledge is not within the creature's power at all
D) Because the definition of necessary knowledge only applies to sensory knowledge

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge is not necessary because it can be avoided before acquisition, even if unavoidable afterward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between knowledge that is unavoidable (necessary) and knowledge you can avoid by simply not reasoning. Think about your belief in Allah: is it a necessary knowledge you cannot deny, or a theoretical knowledge you chose to acquire? How would your life change if you stopped engaging in reasoning about Him? Are you actively choosing to know, or just assuming without reflection?
?
The objection claimed that once theoretical knowledge is acquired, it becomes unavoidable. Reflect on a core Islamic belief you once questioned but now hold firmly: did you arrive at it through reasoning or was it always obvious? If it is theoretical, you could have avoided it—but you didn't. What drove you to pursue that knowledge? Is that same drive present in your current learning, or have you become complacent?

What Is Kalam?

Al-Qadi's Definition of Acquired Knowledge
Al-Qadi defines acquired knowledge as that whose acquisition is not within power of the creature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's definition of acquired knowledge and how it avoids potential counterexamples.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is defined by its acquisition being beyond the creature's power.

In this passage, al-Qadi presents his definition of acquired knowledge, clarifying that it is not within the creature's power to acquire. The commentary explains why this definition holds and why objections regarding infinite objects are unfounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation without analysis. Let the author's words establish the definition.
2
Analyze the definition and scope
Notice that al-Qadi defines acquired knowledge as that whose acquisition is not within the creature's power. The commentary clarifies that this excludes knowledge of infinite objects like numbers and shapes.
The definition focuses on the act of acquisition, not the object's infinity.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The passage then justifies the definition by explaining that power implies ability for both the act and its opposite. If acquisition were within power, then refraining would also be within power, which contradicts the definition.
This reasoning repels the objection that the entailment fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول نحن في تلخيص تعريف القاضي هو ما لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً للمخلوق. قوله : ( هو ما لا يكون تحصيله إلخ) أي العلم الحادث الذي لا يكون إلخ، فلا يرد العلم بالأمور الغير المتناهية كالأعداد والأشكال. قوله : (فإذا لم يكن تحصيله إلخ) وذلك لأنه لا معنى للقدرة إلا التمكن من الطرفين، فإذا كان التحصيل مقدوراً يكون تركه الذي هو التحصيل مقدوراً، فاندفع ما توهم من منع الملازمة.
And we say, in summary of al-Qadi's definition: It is that whose acquisition is not within power of the creature. His saying: (That whose acquisition etc.) meaning the contingent knowledge that is not etc. So it does not refute knowledge of infinite things like numbers and shapes. His saying: (For if its acquisition is not etc.) because there is no meaning to power except the ability for both sides. So if acquisition is within power, then leaving it, which is its acquisition, is within power. So what was imagined of preventing the entailment is repelled.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents al-Qadi's definition of acquired knowledge and explains it. The definition states that acquired knowledge is that whose acquisition is not within the power of the creature. The commentator clarifies that this definition avoids a potential counterexample: knowledge of infinite objects like numbers and shapes, which is not fully within our power to acquire, does not contradict the definition because it is not about acquisition. Then he explains the logic: power implies the ability to do both the act and its opposite. If acquisition were within power, then refraining from acquisition would also be within power, but the definition denies that. Thus, the definition is consistent and prevents the imagined objection that the entailment fails.

Key Terms
قادر
تحصيل
مقدور
تعريف القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition includes knowledge of infinite objects, but it is about the act of acquisition, not the object's infinity.
Correction: The definition focuses on whether the creature can bring about the acquisition, not on the infinite nature of the object known.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi's definition, what does it mean for acquired knowledge to be not within power of the creature?
A) Its acquisition is within power.
B) Its acquisition is not within power.
C) Its separation is not within power.
D) It is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi defines acquired knowledge as that whose acquisition is not within the creature's power, and this definition withstands objections regarding infinite objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the acquisition of knowledge is not within your power, how does that challenge your pride in your own learning? Are you willing to see your understanding as a gift rather than an achievement?
?
Do you treat the knowledge you have as something you have earned by your own effort, or as something granted? What would change if you lived with this definition in mind?
?
Can you accept that even your ability to ponder this lesson is not ultimately within your control? How would that affect your humility before God when you seek knowledge?

Definition of Necessary Knowledge and Sensation Example
Al-Iji defines necessary knowledge and responds to an objection about unknown conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessary knowledge is defined as that which is not acquired by one's power, using the example of sensory knowledge, and how an objection about judging unknown conditions is addressed.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge depends on conditions beyond our power, not on mere effort.

Al-Iji begins the second purpose by dividing contingent knowledge into necessary and acquired. He defines necessary knowledge as that whose attainment is not in one's power, then illustrates it with sensory perception. This lesson unpacks that definition, the example, and the key objection that arises: how can we claim something is beyond our power when we do not even know the conditions involved? The passage clarifies that the path to knowledge can be within our power even if the conditions are unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the definition and the example of sensations.
Pay attention to the objection and response.
2
Identify the objection
Notice the objection: if the conditions are unknown, how can we judge them as not in our power? Locate this in the text.
3
Understand the response
The response argues that knowledge is in our power through its path (nazar) even if the conditions are unknown. Consider the role of God's custom.
Reflect on how this resolves the objection.

الكون في مكانه بإجماع منا ومن المعتزلة مع أنه لا سبيل له إلى الانفكاك عن مقدوره، اللهم إلا أن يجعل القاضي خارجاً عن هذا الإجماع وقد يجاب عن المنع المذكور، بأن غير المقدور ينتفي مع انتفاء المقدور فتكون بطريق جري العادة دون الإيجاب ،والتوليد على أن زمان الحصول حينئذ معلوم، وقولهم لا يعلم متى حصلت هي يدل على مجهوليته . قوله : (بل تتوقف على أمور غير مقدورة لا نعلم ما هي فيه بحث وهو أن الحكم على غير The third observatory - the second purpose: Contingent knowledge divides into necessary and acquired. [Necessary is] that whose attainment is not in one's power, such as sensations by the external senses, for they are not obtained by mere sensation which is in our power, but depend on things not in our power which we do not know what they are. His statement: '(they are not obtained by mere sensation)' otherwise error would occur. His statement: '(but they depend on...)' when those things are realized along with sensation, knowledge occurs, otherwise not. And those things are not in our power because power relates only to what is known, and those things are other than known. So his statement 'we do not know' is a resumptive sentence explaining that those things are not in our power, not a description of 'things' as one might think. Then it was objected: if they are not known, how can one judge about the unknown that it is not in our power? His statement: '(for they are obtained...)' meaning their occurrence depends on theoretical reflection which is in our power in terms of existence and non-existence, so they are in our power, since the meaning of knowledge being in our power is only that its path is in our power, and this does not contradict its dependence on the conception of necessary termini, so ponder, for feet have slipped regarding it. 'One who encompasses' from 'that the same' because it is refuted by what he mentioned in the seventh purpose of the fourth type of psychic qualities, building from all its sides such that one is incapable of turning from one direction to another, for he is then able to be in his place by consensus of us and the Mu'tazila, even though he has no way to disengage from what is in his power, unless al-Qadi is excluded from this consensus. And it may be answered to the mentioned prevention that non-makdur is negated with the negation of the makdur, so the complete cause for disengagement from knowledge is the combination of negation of the makdur and the non-makdur, not each of them individually, otherwise impossible concurrence would follow as will come, God willing. And the composite of makdur and non-makdur is not makdur, so disengagement in the mentioned form is not makdur at all. You are aware that the claim of absolute negation of the non-makdur upon negation of the makdur without establishing it is extremely difficult. How can this be, since it is based on the occurrence of non-makdur things after averting the sense, for example? Then attainment would then also be makdur because God's custom is to bring them into existence after averting the sense which is in our power, even if there is no necessitation, just as theoretical knowledge is in our power because it occurs after theoretical reflection which is in our power, even if by way of custom without necessitation or generation. And that the time of occurrence is then known, and their statement 'they do not know when it occurred' indicates its unknowability. His statement: '(but they depend on things not in our power which we do not know what they are)' contains a discussion: how can one judge about the unknown that it is non-makdur, given that it may be in our power even if we have not discovered its makdur path? This is supported by his later statement that it is possible that there is another path for acquisition other than theoretical reflection which is in our power even if we have not discovered it, so reflect.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) as that whose attainment is not in our power, providing the example of sensory knowledge from external senses. The commentary explains that sensation alone is insufficient because knowledge depends on unknown conditions beyond our control. An objection is raised: how can we judge something we do not know as not being in our power? The response argues that the occurrence of such knowledge depends on theoretical reflection (nazar) which is in our power, so knowledge becomes in our power through its path. Further discussion addresses the possibility that even if the conditions are not in our power, the overall cause for disengagement may be composite, and the custom of God in producing knowledge after averting the sense makes it effectively in our power. The passage ends with a caution about judging unknown things as non-makdur.

مكتسب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge depends on unknown things, it cannot be judged as non-makdur.
Correction: The passage explains that knowledge can be in our power through its path (nazar) even if the conditions are unknown, and the unknown conditions may be part of a composite cause not entirely in our power.

Checkpoint
Why is sensory knowledge considered necessary (daruri) according to this passage?
A) Because it is acquired by mere sensation which is in our power.
B) Because it depends on things beyond our power that we do not know.
C) Because it is obtained by theoretical reflection.
D) Because it is impossible to disengage from it.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge is defined by its dependence on conditions beyond our power, yet its attainment can be linked to a path within our power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that necessary knowledge depends on conditions you do not know. How often do you assume that your knowledge is entirely your own achievement, forgetting the hidden factors that Allah provides? What would change if you started every act of learning with humility and reliance on Allah?

The passage shows that the path of theoretical reflection is within your power. Are you taking advantage of this gift? Where in your life have you neglected to reflect, relying instead on mere habit or emotion? What one specific step will you take today to engage in intentional nazar?

Consider the objection raised: how can we judge about unknown matters? The answer points to the known path. In your own life, are there areas where you have not sought knowledge because you assumed it was inaccessible, when in fact the path is open if you would only take it? What is that path for you now?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

The Definition of Theoretical Knowledge (al-Nazari)
Al-Amidi defines theoretical knowledge as that which correct reasoning entails, with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of theoretical knowledge in kalam, including the conditions of inseparability and exclusivity, and why it is defined as entailment rather than necessitation.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge is what correct reasoning entails — neither caused by it nor merely following it.

Al-Amidi's definition of theoretical knowledge (al-nazari) is deceptively simple: 'that which correct reasoning entails.' But what does 'entailment' mean? In this passage, al-Amidi unpacks the term, distinguishing it from causal necessitation and from mere temporal succession. He shows that the definition must capture both that reasoning cannot be separated from this knowledge and that the knowledge does not occur without reasoning. This careful framing reflects the Ash'ari commitment to habitual, not necessary, causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and core terms
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key phrase 'ma yatadammanahu al-nazar al-sahih' (ما يتضمنه النظر الصحيح). Identify the terms 'tadamun', 'ijab', 'tawlid', and 'adah'.
The definition is deceptively simple; the commentary reveals its depth.
2
Understand why 'necessitate' and 'occur after' were rejected
The author explains that they did not say 'what reasoning necessitates' because the Ash'ari school denies causal necessity—knowledge only occurs by habit. They also avoided 'what occurs after reasoning' because that would include some necessary knowledge that follows reasoning but is not theoretical.
This is a key difference from other theological schools.
3
Grasp the two conditions of tadamun
The entailment (tadamun) means both: (1) correct reasoning cannot be free from this knowledge (inseparability), and (2) this knowledge does not occur except with reasoning (exclusivity). The example of second-order knowledge (knowing that you know) tests these conditions.
A perfect entailment requires both conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظري فهو ما يتضمنه النظر الصحيح) هذه عبارة القاضي قال: الآمدي معنى تضمنه له أنهما بحال لو قدر انتفاء الآفات، وأضداد العلم لم ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه بلا إيجاب وتوليد مع أنه لا يحصل إلا معه (ولم نقل ما يوجبه) النظر الصحيح كما قاله بعضهم (إذ ليس إيجاب النظر للعلم مذهبنا) بل حصوله عقيبه بطريق العادة عندنا ولم نقل أيضا (ما يحصل عقيبه إذا يدخل في الحد) حينئذ (بعض الضروريات) أعني ما يحصل من الضروريات عقيب النظر الصحيح، كالعلم بما يحدث به من الألم واللذة والفرح والغم، ونحو ذلك. قوله: (بالقدرة الحادثة) هذا القيد لإخراج العلم الضروري لأنه مقدور التحصيل لنا بالقدرة القديمة. قوله: (لم ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه خرج به العلم بما يحدث به من الألم واللذة والفرح فإنه ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه. قوله: (مع أنه لا يحصل إلا معه متعلق بلم ينفك أي مع عدم انفكاك النظر عنه يكون مختصاً حصوله بالنظر خرج به العلم بالعلم بالشيء الحاصل عقيب النظر، لكنه لا اختصاص له بالنظر لكونه تابعاً للعلم بالشيء، سواء كان العلم بالشيء حاصلاً بالنظر أو بدونه، ولا يخفى أن تضمن الشيء للشيء على وجه الكمال إنما يكون إذا كان كذلك، فلا يرد أن دلالة التضمن على القيدين خفية. تناف ظاهر، اللهم إلا أن يمنع كون الالتفات مقدوراً بناء على أنه لو كان كذلك، لاحتاج إلى التفات آخر وهلم جراً، أو يقال: الأمور البديهية الحاصلة بالالتفات الكائن في وقت دون وقت موقوف على أمور غير مقدورة أيضاً، وقوله: من غير استعانة بحس إلخ، كالتفسير لقوله بمجرد التفاته إليه، وغيره في قوله من غير استعانة بحس أو غيره محمول على الغير من الأمور المقدورة كالنظر والتجربة فتأمل. قوله: (بالقدرة الحادثة) هذا القيد لزيادة التوضيح لا للاحتراز عن العلم القديم، لخروجه بالمقدور تحصيله سواء أريد بالعلم القديم الصفة القديمة، أو تعلقاتها أما على الأول فلأنها بطريق الإيجاب، كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى، وأما على الثاني فلما صرحوا به من وجوب تعلقها بكل ما يجوز تعلقها به. قوله: فهو ما يتضمنه النظر (الصحيح أي علم يتضمنه النظر الصحيح بطريق أن يترتب عليه فلا نقض بالمعدومات، ثم المراد بالتضمن هو الحصول الكلي القطعي على ما ذكره الآمدي، فلا يبطل طرد التعريف بالضروري الحاصل عقيب النظر، كالعلم بأن لنا لذة في هذا النظر لكن يرد على القاضي العلم بالعلم الحاصل عقيب النظر، فإن الأول لا ينفك عن الثاني عنده كما سيأتي في موقف الأعراض إلا أن يلتزم كونه نظرياً كما نقل عن الرازي ولا يخفى بطلانه أو يقيد الترتب بوجه مخصوص.
And as for the theoretical (al-nazari), it is that which correct reasoning (al-nazar al-sahih) entails (yatadammanahu). This is the wording of al-Qadi. Al-Amidi said: The meaning of its entailment is that they are in a state such that, if one posits the absence of defects and opposites of knowledge, correct reasoning would not be free from it, without necessitation (ijab) or generation (tawlid), while it does not occur except with it. (And we did not say what necessitates) correct reasoning, as some said, (for necessitation of reasoning for knowledge is not our doctrine), rather its occurrence after it is by way of habit (al-‘adah) according to us. And we also did not say (what occurs after it, for then some necessary things would enter into the definition) i.e., those necessary things that occur after correct reasoning, like knowledge of the pain, pleasure, joy, and grief that occur to one, and the like. His statement: (by acquired power (al-qudra al-haditha))—this qualifier is to exclude necessary knowledge, because it is attainable for us by eternal power. His statement: (correct reasoning is not free from it)—it excludes knowledge of the pain, pleasure, and joy that occur, for correct reasoning is free from it. His statement: (while it does not occur except with it) is related to “is not free”; i.e., along with the non-separation of reasoning from it, its occurrence is specific to reasoning. This excludes knowledge of the knowledge of a thing that occurs after reasoning, but it has no specificity to reasoning because it follows knowledge of the thing, whether knowledge of the thing occurs by reasoning or without it. It is not hidden that the entailment of a thing for a thing in a perfect manner only exists when it is so; thus it is not objected that the indication of entailment on the two conditions is hidden. This is an apparent conflict, unless it is prevented that attention (iltifat) is possible based on the fact that if it were so, it would need another attention ad infinitum, or it is said: the self-evident matters (umur badihiyya) obtained by attention occurring at a particular time depend on matters that are also not possible. And his statement: (without assistance from sense, etc.) is an interpretation of his statement: (by merely attending to it). And his statement: (without assistance from sense or other) is interpreted as other than the possible matters like reasoning and experience, so reflect. His statement: (by acquired power)—this qualifier is for further clarification, not to exclude eternal knowledge, because it is excluded by ‘attainable’, whether by eternal knowledge is meant the eternal attribute or its relations. As for the first, because they are by way of necessitation, as will come, God willing. And as for the second, because they have stated the necessity of their relation to everything to which they may relate. His statement: (so it is that which correct reasoning entails) i.e., knowledge that correct reasoning entails in the manner of being consequent upon it; thus it is not refuted by nonexistents. Then the intended meaning of entailment (tadamun) is absolute certain occurrence, according to what al-Amidi mentioned. Thus the definition is not invalidated by the necessary knowledge that occurs after reasoning, like the knowledge that we have pleasure in this reasoning. But an objection arises against al-Qadi regarding knowledge of knowledge that occurs after reasoning, for the first does not separate from the second according to him, as will come in the section on accidents, unless he commits to it being theoretical, as transmitted from al-Razi—its invalidity is not hidden—or he restricts the consequence to a specific manner.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the term 'al-nazari' (theoretical knowledge). It begins by presenting the definition from al-Qadi: 'theoretical knowledge is that which correct reasoning entails.' The commentator then explains al-Amidi's interpretation of this definition, focusing on the term 'tadamun' (entailment). The entailment means that if we assume the absence of obstacles and contrary knowledge, correct reasoning cannot be separated from that knowledge—neither by necessitation (ijab) nor by generation (tawlid), but simply by way of habit (‘adah). The definition avoids saying that reasoning 'necessitates' knowledge, because the Ash‘ari school rejects necessary causation. It also avoids saying 'what occurs after it' because that would include some necessary knowledge (e.g., knowledge of pain after seeing something) which is not theoretical. The commentary then explains the qualifiers in the definition: 'acquired power' excludes necessary knowledge (which is attainable by eternal power), and the condition 'correct reasoning is not free from it' excludes things like pain that can occur without reasoning. The phrase 'while it does not occur except with it' ensures that the knowledge is specific to reasoning, thereby excluding knowledge of knowledge (e.g., knowing that you know something after reasoning), because that kind of second-order knowledge does not depend specifically on reasoning—it could occur even without reasoning. The commentator then addresses a potential objection about the subtlety of 'tadamun' indicating both conditions, noting that a perfect entailment requires both inseparability and exclusivity. The discussion continues with a clarification about the qualifier 'acquired power' being merely explanatory, not to exclude eternal knowledge. Finally, the definition is refined: 'entailment' means absolute certain occurrence, and the definition is not invalidated by necessary knowledge that follows reasoning (e.g., the pleasure from reasoning itself). However, an objection arises regarding knowledge of knowledge that follows reasoning—does it become theoretical? The commentator notes that some, like al-Razi, treat it as theoretical, but this is considered invalid, or one must restrict the consequence in a specific way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'tadamun' means simple logical implication, but here it includes both inseparability and exclusivity, with emphasis on habitual occurrence rather than causal necessitation.
Correction: Tadamun in this context means that correct reasoning cannot be separated from the knowledge (inseparability) and that the knowledge does not occur except with reasoning (exclusivity), all while denying any causal necessitation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author not define theoretical knowledge as 'what correct reasoning necessitates' (ما يوجبه)?
A) Because reasoning cannot produce knowledge at all.
B) Because the Ash‘ari school denies that reasoning necessarily causes knowledge; it occurs only by habit.
C) Because 'necessitation' would include necessary knowledge.
D) Because the definition would then be circular.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge is not caused by reasoning but habitually follows it, under the conditions that reasoning cannot be free from it and it does not occur without reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your most certain beliefs as if they are forced upon you by evidence alone, or do you acknowledge that they are gifts that habitually arise when you engage in correct reasoning? How would this shift your humility?
?
If theoretical knowledge is not caused by your effort but only follows it by habit, are you willing to invest in deep reflection even when the outcome is not guaranteed? What holds you back from such trust?
?
The passage warns against conflating knowledge that occurs after reasoning with theoretical knowledge. Is your confidence rooted more in the process you followed or in the truth itself? How can you ensure your certainty is in the object, not the method?

What Is Kalam?
