اللفظ ليصح مقابلته بقوله، ولا شيء مما يصدق عليه فإن هذا للذات، إلا أن المراد به ولا شيء مما يصدق عليه من حيث إنه يصدق عليه، والقرينة على هذا التقدير ما تقرر من أن العام إذا قوبل بالخاص يراد به ما عدا الخاص، ووقع في بعض النسخ ولا بشيء مما يصدق عليه، فهو تقدير للمعطوف . قوله : ( فإن الوجه المجهول فرضاً هو الذات إلخ) أشار بقوله فرضاً إلى أن اعتبار مجهولية الذات. بطريق التمثيل اهتماما بشأن ما هو الأهم أعني اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة، وفي شرح المقاصد مجهولية الذات لازمةً فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو علم الشيء بحقيقته، وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا بالتعريف انتهى وهذا بناء على أن تصور الشيء بحقيقته حصول الشيء بنفسه، فلا يمكن طلب حصوله باعتبار حصول أمر عارض له إذ الشيء إذا كان حاضراً لا يطلب بشيء آخر يكون آلة لحضوره، فليس المطلوب إلا ثبوت ذلك العارض له وكونه وجهاً من وجوهه فماله التصديق . قوله : ( بكهنه ولا شيء مما يصدق عليه الواقع في بعض نسخ المتن، ولا بشيء بالباء الجارة، ولهذا قدر الشارح لفظ بكنهه تعييناً لما عطف عليه قوله ولا بشيء وفي بعض النسخ، ولا شيء بالرفع عطفاً على ذاته فيتوجه على ظاهره أنه يشعر بعدم الفرق بين العلم بالوجه، والعلم بالشيء من ذلك الوجه لأنه جعل تصور الوجه الصادق على الشيء منافياً لمجهوليته المطلقة وليس المنافي لها إلا تصوره ولو ،بوجه والتوجيه أن مراده شيء مما يصدق عليه من حيث إنه يصدق عليه فيتم التقريب قوله : ( فإن الوجه المجهول فرضاً هو الذات والحقيقة قال في شرح المقاصد: هذا تحقيق لما هو المهم أعني إمكان اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة وتنبيه على أن مجهولية الذات لازمة فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو علم الشيء بحقيقته وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا بالتعريف وفي المنبه عليه بحث ظاهر إذ اكتساب بعض العوارض للشيء بعد معرفة حقيقته، قد يكون من حيث إنه آلة لملاحظته ومرآة يتعرف حاله به، فيكون المطلوب التصور دون التصديق وكون التصور بالعارض أنقص من التصور بالكنية لا ينافي كون الأول مطلوباً قد قد يتعلق به الغرض دون الثاني والأولى أن تعيين جهة المجهولية للذات لكونها أغلب وأنسب لمن هو بصدد معرفة حقائق الأشياء، فلذا حمل الشارح الذات في عبارة المصنف على الحقيقة ولم يحمله على ذات المطلوب حتى يشمل أنواع التعريفات كما سيأتي مثله على أن فيه تنبيها على التوجيهين. مخصوصة هذه الأمور المذكورة صفاته فتطلب تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة بعينها لتصور بكنهها أو بوجه أتم مما ذكر وإن لم يبلغ الكنه
And nothing that is predicated of it from its essential or accidental attributes (and this unknown aspect is not like that, rather something may be imagined that is predicated of it, and it is the known aspect. For the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence and reality whose conceptualization is sought in its inner reality, and the known aspect is some of the established considerations that are true of it, whether essential or accidental, as the spirit is known, for example, as a thing with which there is life, sense, and motion, and that it has a reality.) Also conceptualization. (His statement: 'by its inner reality': he estimated this phrase to make it correspond to his statement 'and nothing that is predicated of it', for this is for the essence, except that what is meant is 'and nothing that is predicated of it insofar as it is predicated of it'. The evidence for this estimation is what has been established that when the general is contrasted with the specific, what is other than the specific is intended. And in some copies it is 'and not with something that is predicated of it', so it is an estimation of the coordinated item. His statement: 'For the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence, etc.': by his saying 'hypothetically' he indicated that considering the unknownness of the essence is by way of exemplification, emphasizing what is most important, namely acquiring conceptualization according to reality. And in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'The unknownness of the essence is necessary in what is sought to be conceptualized, even if the thing is known in its reality, and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that is through proof, not through definition.' End. This is based on the fact that conceptualizing a thing in its reality is obtaining the thing itself, so it is impossible to seek its attainment by considering the attainment of an accident that accedes to it, for if the thing is present, it is not sought by something else that would be an instrument for its presence. Thus what is sought is only the establishment of that accident for it and its being an aspect of its aspects, so it belongs to assent. His statement: 'by its inner reality and nothing that is predicated of it' occurring in some copies of the text, and 'not with something' with the particle ba', and therefore the commentator estimated the phrase 'by its inner reality' to specify what is coordinated with his statement 'and not with something'. And in some copies, 'and nothing' in the nominative case, coordinated with 'its essence', so it apparently suggests that there is no difference between knowledge of the aspect and knowledge of the thing from that aspect, because he considered the conceptualization of the aspect that is true of the thing as contradictory to its absolute unknownness, but what contradicts it is only its conceptualization, even by an aspect. And the explanation is that he means something that is predicated of it insofar as it is predicated of it, so the approximation is complete. His statement: 'For the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence and reality.' He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'This is a verification of what is important, namely the possibility of acquiring conceptualization according to reality, and an indication that the unknownness of the essence is necessary in what is sought to be conceptualized, so that even if the thing is known in its reality and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that is through proof, not through definition.' And what he warned of is open to discussion, for acquiring some accidents of a thing after knowing its reality may be insofar as it is an instrument for observing it and a mirror by which its state is known, so what is sought is conceptualization, not assent. And that conceptualization by accident is inferior to conceptualization by inner reality does not negate that the former is sought, as the purpose may be attached to it rather than the latter. And it is better to specify the direction of unknownness for the essence because it is more prevalent and more appropriate for someone who is in the process of knowing the realities of things. Thus the commentator interpreted 'essence' in the author's statement as 'reality' and did not interpret it as the essence of the sought thing so that it would include the types of definitions, as will come similarly, and it contains a warning of the two directions. (These mentioned matters are His attributes, so that specific reality is sought itself to be conceptualized in its inner reality or by a more complete aspect than what was mentioned, even if it does not reach the inner reality.)
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the distinction between the known aspect (al-wajh al-ma'lum) and the unknown aspect (al-wajh al-majhul) in the context of seeking conceptualization of a thing. It begins by quoting the main text: 'And nothing that is predicated of it from its essential or accidental attributes'—meaning that the unknown aspect is not like that; rather, something can be conceived that is predicated, i.e., the known aspect. The unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence (dhat) or reality (haqiqa) itself, which is sought to be conceptualized in its inner reality (kunn). The known aspect is some established consideration, whether essential or accidental. The commentator then explains the textual variants: the original phrase 'bikunnihi' (by its inner reality) is estimated to contrast with 'and nothing that is predicated of it'. Some copies have 'wa la bi-shay' (and not with something) with the preposition ba', so the commentator supplies 'bikunnihi' to specify the coordinated item. He also discusses the meaning of 'hypothetically' (faradan) as an exemplification to emphasize acquiring conceptualization of the reality. Citing Sharh al-Maqasid, he explains that the unknownness of the essence is necessary so that even if one knows the thing in its reality, acquiring accidents is through proof, not definition. Then he delves into the difference between knowing a thing by its aspect and knowing the aspect itself, noting that some textual variants suggest no difference, but the correct interpretation is that the aspect is predicated of the thing. Finally, he concludes that the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-mukhtassa) is sought for conceptualization by its inner reality or by a more complete aspect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the unknown aspect is completely separate from the thing's essence, or that it is entirely unknowable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, which is sought but not yet attained; it is not a separate entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'unknown aspect' (al-wajh al-majhul) hypothetically identified as?
A) The known aspect
B) The essence and reality of the thing
C) An accidental attribute
D) The instrument of observation

Key Takeaway
The unknown aspect in seeking conceptualization is the essence itself, while the known aspect consists of its attributes, and the goal is to grasp the reality either directly or through more complete aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your knowledge of Allah's attributes (e.g., Ar-Rahman, Al-Alim) ever bring you closer to His essence, or do you merely accumulate descriptions? What would change if you truly sought Him beyond the attributes?
?
When you learn about spiritual realities like the soul or the afterlife, do you settle for knowing 'about' them, or do you hunger to know them with your whole being, even if full comprehension is impossible?
?
The commentator argues that acquiring attributes after knowing the reality can be a mirror for conceptualization. Is your study of Islamic theology a mirror that reflects the truth, or a collection of facts that leaves you unchanged?

What Is Kalam?

The Third Thing and the Distinction Between Two Kinds of Knowledge by Aspect
Enumerates two positions on the distinction between two types of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over a third thing beyond the two aspects and how early and later scholars differ on knowledge of a thing by its aspect versus knowledge of the aspect itself.

Core Idea
The disagreement over a third thing hinges on how 'knowledge by aspect' is analyzed.

This lesson introduces a key debate in Islamic epistemology: when we know something by one of its aspects, is there a third entity beyond the known and unknown aspects that is sought? It then lays out the contrasting positions of early and later scholars on the nature of such knowledge, setting the stage for the responses that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third thing claim
Read the first part of the passage where some scholars affirm a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the two aspects. Note the commentator's caution about the phrasing.
The two aspects are the known (al-wajh al-ma'lum) and the unknown (al-wajh al-majhul).
2
Understand the two types of knowledge
The passage distinguishes between 'knowledge of a thing by its aspect' and 'knowledge of the aspect itself'. Later scholars say they differ in essence; early scholars say they differ by consideration.
This distinction is crucial for the later argument.
3
Compare the two schools
Focus on how each school defines the difference. Later scholars make the aspect itself an instrument; early scholars see the same mental content viewed differently.
The terms taghayyur bi al-dhat and taghayyur bi al-i'tibar are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من أثبت في جواب هذه الشبهة وراء الوجهين أي الوجه المعلوم والوجه المجهول أمراً ثالثاً هو المطلوب يقومان به أي الوجهان به، وهذا القيد أعني قيام الوجهين بالأمر الثالث زائد على كلام هذا المثبت وفيه حزازة لجواز أن يكون أحد الوجهين [؟] قال: من قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ الأول الحاصل في الذهن نفس الوجه، وهو آلة ملاحظة الشيء والشيء معلوم بالذات، وفي الثاني الحاصل في الذهن صورة الوجه، وهو المعلوم بالذات من غير التفات إلى الشيء ذي الوجه، وقال المتقدمون : التغاير بينهما بالاعتبار إذ لا شك في أنه لا يمكن أن يشاهد بالضاحك أمر سواه، إلا أنه إذا عتبر صدقه عليه أمر واتحاده معه كما في موضوع القضية المحصورة كان علم الشيء بالوجه، وإذا اعتبر مع قطع النظر عن ذلك كان علم الوجه كما في موضوع القضية الطبيعية، إذا علمت هذا، فاعلم أن عود الإمام إما مبني على عدم التغاير مطلقا، وتقريره أن الشيء المشعور به من وجه دون وجه لا يطلب بوجهيه لأن الوجه المعلوم معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول فلا يطلب شيء منهما ، فلا يمكن طلبه وإما مبني على رأي المتقدمين وتقريره أن المطلوب إذا كان مشعوراً به بوجه دون وجه كان المعلوم والمجهول في الحقيقة هما الوجهان، لكن من حيث صدقهما على ذلك الشيء، واتحادهما به والمعلوم معلوم مطلقاً، والمجهول مجهول مطلقاً لا يمكن طلب شيء منهما، فإن أجيب علي رأي المتقدمين فالجواب ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً، لأنه إذا كان الوجه المعلوم معلوماً من حيث الاتحاد بذلك الشيء، والمجهول مجهولاً من تلك الحيثية كان الوجه المجهول معلوماً من حيث اتحاد الوجه المعلوم به ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب نقد المحصل إذ المطلوب ليس أمرا ثالثا عندهم، وإن أجيب على رأي المتأخرين فالجواب ما ذكره في النقد وهو أنه لا يلزم من امتناع طلب الوجهين امتناع طلب الأمر الثالث الذي هو ذو الوجهين، فكما أن الوجه المعلوم صار آلة الملاحظة الشيء، ومرآة لانكشافه كذلك، يطلب ذلك الشيء بأن يصير أمر آخر آلة لملاحظته، ومرآة له وتفصيله أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه، فيكون العارض معلوماً، والشيء مغفولاً عنه بالكلية، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظته، وحينئذ يكون معلوماً باعتبار ذلك العارض مجهولاً باعتبار آخر، فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى، ولا استمالة فيه ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب المصنف، إذ ليس المطلوب عندهم الوجه حتى يجاب بأن الوجه المجهول ليس مجهولاً مطلقاً، فتدبر والله الموفق وأما ما ذكره الشارح من أنه الزام للإمام حيث اعترف بمغايرة الوجهين لذي الوجهين ففيه إن العبارة المشعرة بالتغاير ليس إلا قوله : لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد، إذ لا بد من التغاير بين الظرف والمظروف، وهو لا يقتضي التغاير بالذات لجواز قوله : ( أحد الوجهين جزءاً (الخ في كونه جزءاً كفاية في أن القيام ها هنا غير واقع موقعه، فلهذا اقتصر عليه، وإلا فيجوز أن يكون كلا الوجهين جزءاً
And among them is one who affirmed, in answer to this ambiguity, besides the two aspects—i.e., the known aspect and the unknown aspect—a third thing that is sought, and the two aspects subsist through it. This restriction, namely the subsistence of the two aspects through the third thing, is additional to the statement of this affirmer, and there is a difficulty in it because it is possible that one of the two aspects [؟] He said: from his statement: 'And among them is one who affirmed, etc.' Know that they differed concerning knowledge of a thing by its aspect and knowledge of the aspect of a thing; there is no verification that there is no distinction between them at all. The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) said: distinction in essence, for the first—what occurs in the mind—is the aspect itself, and it is an instrument for observing the thing, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, what occurs in the mind is the form of the aspect, and it is what is known in essence without attending to the thing that has the aspect. The early scholars (al-mutaqaddimun) said: distinction between them is by consideration, for there is no doubt that it is impossible to observe by 'the laughing' something other than it, except that if one considers its truth regarding something and its union with it, as in the subject of a quantified proposition, it is knowledge of the thing by its aspect; and if one considers with disregard of that, it is knowledge of the aspect, as in the subject of a natural proposition. If you know this, then know that the return of the Imam is either based on absolute non-distinction, and its explanation: the thing perceived by one aspect without another is not sought by its two aspects because the known aspect is known and the unknown aspect is unknown, so nothing from them is sought, thus it cannot be sought; or based on the view of the early scholars, and its explanation: if what is sought is perceived by one aspect without another, then what is known and what is unknown in reality are the two aspects, but insofar as they are true of that thing and their union with it, and what is known is absolutely known, and what is unknown is absolutely unknown, nothing from them can be sought. If one answers according to the view of the early scholars, the answer is what the author mentioned: we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, because if the known aspect is known from the perspective of union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that perspective, then the unknown aspect is known from the perspective of the union of the known aspect with it, and there is no meaning then to the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, for what is sought with them is not a third thing. If one answers according to the view of the later scholars, the answer is what he mentioned in al-Naqd: it does not follow from the impossibility of seeking the two aspects the impossibility of seeking the third thing which is the possessor of the two aspects. For just as the known aspect became an instrument for observing the thing and a mirror for its disclosure, likewise that thing is sought by making another thing an instrument for its observation and a mirror for it. Its detail: the accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident is known and the thing is completely neglected; and it may be made an instrument for its observation, and then it is known by virtue of that accident and unknown by virtue of another, so the known and the unknown are unified, but it is known from one perspective and unknown from another perspective. There is no impossibility in that, and there is no meaning then to the author's answer, for what is sought with them is not the aspect so that it could be answered that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. As for what the commentator mentioned, that it is an obligation upon the Imam since he acknowledged the difference between the two aspects and the possessor of the two aspects, there is in it that the expression indicating difference is only his statement: 'but when they meet in one thing', for there must be difference between the container and the contained, and that does not necessitate difference in essence because it is possible his statement: 'one of the two aspects is a part'—that it is a part suffices that the subsistence here is not in its proper place, so he limited himself to it, otherwise it is possible that both aspects are a part.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses a response to a doubt (shubha) concerning the possibility of seeking conceptualization of a thing when it is known only by an aspect. Some scholars posited a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the known and unknown aspects as the object of seeking. The commentator notes that restricting the two aspects to subsisting through this third thing is an addition to the affirmer's statement and has a difficulty (hazaza) because one of the aspects might be a part. He then explains the disagreement between the early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars regarding knowledge of a thing by its aspect ('ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and knowledge of the aspect itself ('ilm wajh al-shay). The later scholars affirm essential distinction (taghayyur bi al-dhat): in the first, the aspect itself is in the mind as an instrument, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, the form of the aspect is known in essence without attending to the thing. The early scholars say distinction is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar): the same mental content can be viewed as knowledge of the thing by its aspect when considering its truth and union with the thing, or as knowledge of the aspect when disregarding that. The commentator then analyzes two bases for the Imam's argument: either absolute non-distinction, leading to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its aspects, or the early scholars' view, leading to the same impossibility. The author's response (al-Musannif) is then given: for the early scholars, he denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because union with the known aspect makes it known from that perspective. For the later scholars, the response from al-Naqd is that seeking the two aspects is not the same as seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects); just as the known aspect serves as an instrument, another instrument can be sought for the thing. The commentator concludes by reflecting on the commentator's (al-Sharh) point about the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor.

المتأخرون
آلة الملاحظة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third thing is an external entity distinct from the known and unknown aspects.
Correction: The third thing is a conceptual posit; the passage focuses on its logical role, not its ontology.

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun), how do 'knowledge of a thing by its aspect' and 'knowledge of the aspect itself' differ?
A) They are identical in essence
B) They differ by consideration only
C) They differ in essence: one is the aspect itself in the mind, the other is the form of the aspect
D) They differ because one is sought and the other is not

Key Takeaway
The dispute over a third thing and the nature of knowledge by aspect is foundational for the subsequent argument about the possibility of seeking an unknown thing.

Responses Based on Early and Later Views and the Commentator's Point
Analyzes two responses to the Imam's argument and a commentator's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam's argument against seeking a thing by its two aspects is answered differently based on early and later views, and why the commentator's objection differs.

Core Idea
The impossibility of seeking the two aspects does not entail impossibility of seeking the thing itself.

This lesson continues the discussion by presenting the Imam's argument based on two possible assumptions (non-distinction or early view) and then shows how each school provides a counter-response. It concludes with the commentator's remark on the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor. The passage requires careful attention to the different levels of analysis.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two bases of the Imam's argument
The Imam's argument can be based either on absolute non-distinction or on the early scholars' view. Read how each leads to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its two aspects.
The two aspects are the known and unknown.
2
Follow the answer for the early view
The author (al-Musannif) denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because it is known through its union with the known aspect.
This response avoids needing a third thing.
3
Follow the answer for the later view
From al-Naqd: the impossibility of seeking the two aspects does not rule out seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects). The known aspect serves as an instrument to seek the thing via another instrument.
This preserves the possibility of seeking the thing itself.
4
Understand the commentator's point
The commentator (al-Sharh) argues that the Imam acknowledged a difference between the aspects and their possessor, but this does not force essential distinction.
The container-contained relationship can be conceptual.

ومنهم من أثبت في جواب هذه الشبهة وراء الوجهين أي الوجه المعلوم والوجه المجهول أمراً ثالثاً هو المطلوب يقومان به أي الوجهان به، وهذا القيد أعني قيام الوجهين بالأمر الثالث زائد على كلام هذا المثبت وفيه حزازة لجواز أن يكون أحد الوجهين [؟] قال: من قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ العارض مجهولاً باعتبار آخر، فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى، ولا استمالة فيه ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب المصنف، إذ ليس المطلوب عندهم الوجه حتى يجاب بأن الوجه المجهول ليس مجهولاً مطلقاً، فتدبر والله الموفق وأما ما ذكره الشارح من أنه الزام للإمام حيث اعترف بمغايرة الوجهين لذي الوجهين ففيه إن العبارة المشعرة بالتغاير ليس إلا قوله : لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد، إذ لا بد من جزءاً
And among them is one who affirmed, in answer to this ambiguity, besides the two aspects—i.e., the known aspect and the unknown aspect—a third thing that is sought, and the two aspects subsist through it. This restriction, namely the subsistence of the two aspects through the third thing, is additional to the statement of this affirmer, and there is a difficulty in it because it is possible that one of the two aspects [؟] He said: from his statement: 'And among them is one who affirmed, etc.' Know that they differed concerning knowledge of a thing by its aspect and knowledge of the aspect of a thing; there is no verification that there is no distinction between them at all. The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) said: distinction in essence, for the first—what occurs in the mind—is the aspect itself, and it is an instrument for observing the thing, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, what occurs in the mind is the form of the aspect, and it is what is known in essence without attending to the thing that has the aspect. The early scholars (al-mutaqaddimun) said: distinction between them is by consideration, for there is no doubt that it is impossible to observe by 'the laughing' something other than it, except that if one considers its truth regarding something and its union with it, as in the subject of a quantified proposition, it is knowledge of the thing by its aspect; and if one considers with disregard of that, it is knowledge of the aspect, as in the subject of a natural proposition. If you know this, then know that the return of the Imam is either based on absolute non-distinction, and its explanation: the thing perceived by one aspect without another is not sought by its two aspects because the known aspect is known and the unknown aspect is unknown, so nothing from them is sought, thus it cannot be sought; or based on the view of the early scholars, and its explanation: if what is sought is perceived by one aspect without another, then what is known and what is unknown in reality are the two aspects, but insofar as they are true of that thing and their union with it, and what is known is absolutely known, and what is unknown is absolutely unknown, nothing from them can be sought. If one answers according to the view of the early scholars, the answer is what the author mentioned: we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, because if the known aspect is known from the perspective of union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that perspective, then the unknown aspect is known from the perspective of the union of the known aspect with it, and there is no meaning then to the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, for what is sought with them is not a third thing. If one answers according to the view of the later scholars, the answer is what he mentioned in al-Naqd: it does not follow from the impossibility of seeking the two aspects the impossibility of seeking the third thing which is the possessor of the two aspects. For just as the known aspect became an instrument for observing the thing and a mirror for its disclosure, likewise that thing is sought by making another thing an instrument for its observation and a mirror for it. Its detail: the accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident is known and the thing is completely neglected; and it may be made an instrument for its observation, and then it is known by virtue of that accident and unknown by virtue of another, so the known and the unknown are unified, but it is known from one perspective and unknown from another perspective. There is no impossibility in that, and there is no meaning then to the author's answer, for what is sought with them is not the aspect so that it could be answered that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. As for what the commentator mentioned, that it is an obligation upon the Imam since he acknowledged the difference between the two aspects and the possessor of the two aspects, there is in it that the expression indicating difference is only his statement: 'but when they meet in one thing', for there must be difference between the container and the contained, and that does not necessitate difference in essence because it is possible his statement: 'one of the two aspects is a part'—that it is a part suffices that the subsistence here is not in its proper place, so he limited himself to it, otherwise it is possible that both aspects are a part.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses a response to a doubt (shubha) concerning the possibility of seeking conceptualization of a thing when it is known only by an aspect. Some scholars posited a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the known and unknown aspects as the object of seeking. The commentator notes that restricting the two aspects to subsisting through this third thing is an addition to the affirmer's statement and has a difficulty (hazaza) because one of the aspects might be a part. He then explains the disagreement between the early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars regarding knowledge of a thing by its aspect ('ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and knowledge of the aspect itself ('ilm wajh al-shay). The later scholars affirm essential distinction (taghayyur bi al-dhat): in the first, the aspect itself is in the mind as an instrument, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, the form of the aspect is known in essence without attending to the thing. The early scholars say distinction is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar): the same mental content can be viewed as knowledge of the thing by its aspect when considering its truth and union with the thing, or as knowledge of the aspect when disregarding that. The commentator then analyzes two bases for the Imam's argument: either absolute non-distinction, leading to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its aspects, or the early scholars' view, leading to the same impossibility. The author's response (al-Musannif) is then given: for the early scholars, he denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because union with the known aspect makes it known from that perspective. For the later scholars, the response from al-Naqd is that seeking the two aspects is not the same as seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects); just as the known aspect serves as an instrument, another instrument can be sought for the thing. The commentator concludes by reflecting on the commentator's (al-Sharh) point about the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor.

المتأخرون

آلة الملاحظة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam's argument is unanswerable; no response is possible.
Correction: Both early and later scholars provide answers, showing the argument's premises can be challenged.

Checkpoint
According to the response in al-Naqd for the later view, why does the impossibility of seeking the two aspects not preclude seeking the thing itself?
A) Because the thing itself is already known
B) Because the two aspects are irrelevant
C) Because another thing can serve as an instrument to seek the thing
D) Because the unknown aspect becomes known

Key Takeaway
The argument's success depends on which school's view is assumed, and each school has a way to uphold the possibility of seeking the unknown thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to admit that there is a 'face' of God's essence that is utterly unknown to you, or do you pretend to have full comprehension? How does that affect your pursuit of Him?
?
The later scholars say the known aspect becomes a mirror for the unknown. In your own journey, have you ever used a known attribute of God as a mirror to seek a deeper knowledge of His essence? What concrete step will you take this week to turn a familiar concept into a tool for deeper insight?
?
The commentator's charge of inconsistency forces the Imam to clarify his terms. When someone challenges your belief, do you defensively repeat what you already know, or do you dig deeper into the exact meaning of your words, even if it leads to uncomfortable refinement?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Three Invalid Ways to Define Conceptual Essence
Al-Imam enumerates three invalid ways to define conceptual essence and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conceptual essence cannot be defined by itself, by its part, or by something external, according to al-Imam al-Razi's argument and its refutation.

Core Idea
Defining conceptual essence by itself, its part, or external all lead to impossibility.

This lesson presents the second argument from al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the possibility of acquiring conceptual knowledge through acquisition. He lays out three potential ways to define a conceptual essence and demonstrates that each leads to an impossibility. The passage is dense but crucial for understanding the limits of definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three-way division
The passage divides the possible definers of conceptual essence into three categories: itself, its part, or something external. Note that the third category includes both complete and partial external definers.
This division is exhaustive for any potential definer.
2
Examine the refutation of self-definition
Definition by itself is invalid because it would require knowledge of the definer before knowledge of the defined, leading to circularity. This is self-evidently impossible.
The definer must be known prior to the defined, but here they are identical.
3
Examine the refutation of part-definition
Definition by a part fails because either the part defines itself (circularity) or it defines another part, which is external to it, leading to the problem of external definition.
The commentary also discusses the possibility of defining by all parts, which reduces to self-definition.
4
Examine the refutation of external definition
Definition by something external is invalid unless that external encompasses all individuals uniquely, but even then it faces issues of circularity or infinite regress.
The passage notes that defining 'for' the external is not the same as defining 'by' the external.

الوجه الثاني من متمسكي الإمام في امتناع كسبية التصور أن يقال: ( الماهية ) أي المفهوم التصوري ) أن عرفت ) وحصلت بالكسب والنظر ( فإما بنفسها أو بجزئها أو بالخارج منها سواء كان خارجاً بتمامه أو ببعضه ( والأقسام) بأسرها ( باطلة أما الأول فلأنه يستلزم معرفتها قبل معرفتها لأن معرفة المعرف الموصل متقدمة على المعرف الموصل إليه، وتقدم الشيء على نفسه محال بديهة ( وأما الثاني فلأن جميع الأجزاء نفسها فلا يجوز تعريف الماهية بجميع أجزائها لأنه نقيضه كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه ليس مشعوراً به مطلقاً، وعكسه المستوي بعض ما ليس مشعوراً به مطلقاً لا يمتنع طلبه، وهذا لا ينافي الأصل لجواز صدقه باعتبار أن يكون ذلك البعض مشعوراً به من وجه دون وجه كالمطلوب التصديقي . قوله : (أي المفهوم التصوري) أي ما من شأنه أن يتصور، وفائدة التفسير إخراج المفهوم التصديقي فإن الإمام قائل باكتسابه، والقرينة على ذلك التفسير قوله إن عرفت . قوله: (فإما بنفسها أي من غير تصريح بالجزء، فيخرج عنه التعريف بجميع الأجزاء، ويدخل في قوله بجزئها سواء كان إلخ، فيشمل المركب من الداخل والخارج كالرسم التام . قوله : ( أي المفهوم التصوري فسر الماهية بهذا، وإن كانت شاملة للمفهوم التصديقي لأن النزاع في المفهوم التصوري فإن قلت ماهية الشيء ما به الشيء هو هو سواء، وجد الفاهم أو لم يوجد، وعلى تقدير وجوده فهم أو لم يفهم، فكيف عرفها بالمفهوم؟ قلت : أراد بالمفهوم شأنه أن يتعلق به الفهم لا المفهوم بالفعل. قوله : ( فلأن جميع الأجزاء نفسها فإن قلت : التعريف بالجزء لا يتناول التعريف بجميع الأجزاء لأنها نفس الشيء لا جزؤه، وحمل الجزء على ما ليس بخارج لا يلائم جعله قسيما للتعريف بالنفس، قلت : أراد بالتعريف بالجزء أن يكون الجزء مذكوراً في التعريف صراحة، فيتناول التعريف بجميع الأجزاء ويقال : التعريف بالنفس فتأمل . (المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع : المذاهب الضعيفة في هذه المسألة ١١٧) تعريف للشيء بنفسه ( والبعض) من أجزاء الماهية ( إن عرفها وأنها لا تعرف) بالتخفيف من المعرفة ( إلا بمعرفة جميع الأجزاء عرف ذلك البعض ( نفسه وقد أبطل والخارج أي وعرف الجزء الخارج هو منه ( وسيبطل) وهذان المحذوران إنما يلزمان معاً إذا كان ذلك البعض معرفاً، لكنه الماهية وهو ممنوع، فالأولى أن يقال: والبعض إن عرفها فلا بد أن يعرف جزءاً منها، فذلك الجزء إما نفسه فيكون معرفاً لنفسه، وإما غيره فيلزم التعريف بالخارج، لأن كل جزء خارج عما يقابله من الأجزاء ( وأما الثالث : فلأن الخارج لا يعرف) الماهية ( إلا إذ كان شاملاً لأفرادها دون شيء معرفاً سبباً لمعرفة الماهية وموصلاً إلى تصورها، فلا يكون معرفاً إذ لا معنى للمعرف إلا الموصل. قوله : ( وأما غيره فيلزم التعريف بالخارج) فإن قلت : الجزء المعرف وإن كان غير المعرف، وخارجاً عنه لكن يجوز أن يكون ذلك الجزء المعرف مركباً من المعرف، وغيره فلا يلزم التعريف بالخارج، وإن التجأ عن أن الغير لا يطلق على الكل بالنسبة إلى جزئه يبقى الاحتمال المذكور خارجاً عن القسمين، قلت: لم يلتفت إليه لأنه ينقل الكلام إلى تعريف المركب، فيلزم الانتهاء إلى تعريف الجزء لجزء خارج هو عنه، وأما القول بجواز أن يكون المعرف هو المجموع من حيث هو مجموع لا شيء من أجزائه فهو الجواب الحق على ما سيأتي من الكلام الآتي في تقرير الاعتراض
The second aspect of the Imam's supporters for the impossibility of acquiring conceptual knowledge is to say: The essence (māhiyya), i.e., the conceptual concept, if it is known and obtained by acquisition and consideration, then [it is known] either by itself, or by its part, or by something external to it, whether the external is complete or partial. All the divisions are invalid. As for the first, because it requires knowledge of it before knowledge of it, since knowledge of the means that leads [to the known] is prior to the means that it leads to, and the priority of something over itself is self-evidently impossible. As for the second, because all the parts are itself, so it is not permissible to define the essence by all of its parts, because its contrary is that everything that is not impossible to seek is not a perceived concept absolutely, and its simple converse is that some of what is not a perceived concept absolutely is not impossible to seek, and this does not contradict the original, because it can be true if that some is perceived in one respect and not in another, like the required veridical [proposition]. His statement: (i.e., the conceptual concept) means that which is apt to be conceived, and the benefit of the interpretation is to exclude the veridical concept, for the Imam says it is acquired, and the evidence for that interpretation is his statement 'if it is known'. His statement: (then either by itself) meaning without explicit mention of the part, so defining by all parts is excluded from it, and it enters into his statement 'by its part, whether it is... etc.', so it includes the composite of internal and external like the complete definition. His statement: (i.e., the conceptual concept) he interpreted essence by this, even though it includes the veridical concept, because the dispute is about the conceptual concept. If you say: the essence of a thing is that by which the thing is what it is, whether the understander exists or not, and if he exists, whether he understands or not, so how did he define it by concept? I say: he meant by concept the aptness to be understood, not the actual concept. His statement: (because all the parts are itself) for if you say: definition by part does not include definition by all parts, because they are the thing itself, not its part, and applying 'part' to what is not external does not suit making it a division of definition by itself; I say: he intended by definition by part that the part be mentioned explicitly in the definition, so it includes definition by all parts, and that is called 'definition by itself', so reflect. (The third observatory - fourth aim: the weak schools in this matter) Definition of the thing by itself. And the part of the parts of the essence, if it defines it, and it is not known (with lightening from knowledge) except by knowing all the parts, then that part would define itself, and this has been invalidated, and the external, i.e., and the part external to it defined, and it will be invalidated. These two problems only occur together if that part is a definer of the essence, but it is prevented. So it is better to say: And the part, if it defines it, it must define a part of it, and that part is either itself, so it would be a definer of itself, or other, so definition by the external is necessary, because every part is external to what is other than it. As for the third: because the external does not define the essence unless it encompasses its individuals without anything else. His statement: (and he defined etc.) because what is necessary from the definition of the part for the external is the definition of the thing for the external, and what will be invalidated is definition by the external, and definition for the external does not entail definition by the external, for if the part defines the whole, it is a definition for the external and not by the external, so the commentator made his word 'the external' an attribute that applies to something other than its proper object, so the implied subject refers to the part without the described, and this is based on the Kufan school that requires the pronoun to be manifested when ambiguity is not removed by manifestation, as stated in al-Raḍī. The author of Maqāṣid explained the phrase of the text as tolerance, and perhaps its point is that it is not permissible for one of the two parts to contain the other due to the impossibility of repetition in the essential, so each is external to the other, so definition for the external entails definition by the external here. His statement: (so it must define a part of it) because if it did not define anything of its parts, then the essence would be known… not by all its parts intuitively, or by something else, or unknown, then the supposed definer would not be a definer for it. His statement: (because every part etc.) and it necessitates repetition in the essential, so the essential would not be essential. Except what is other than it. His statement: (encompassing its individuals, known in its coverage and specificity, to be a preference for its consideration for definition without) His statement: (i.e., and the part external to it defined) he diverted the expression from its apparent meaning to repel what is said: what will be invalidated is definition by the external, not for the external. His statement: (so it must define a part of it) because if it did not define anything of the parts, whether they are all known or remain unknown as they were, then what we supposed as a definer would not be a cause for knowing the essence nor a means to conceive it, so it would not be a definer, since there is no meaning of 'definer' except the means. His statement: (and if it is other, then definition by the external is necessary) for if you say: the defining part, even if it is other than the defined and external to it, but it is possible that that defining part is composed of the defined and something else, so definition by the external would not be necessary; and if he insists that 'other' is not applied to the whole in relation to its part, then the mentioned possibility remains outside the two divisions; I say: he did not consider it because it transfers the discussion to the definition of the composite, so one would end up with the definition of a part to a part external to it. And as for the statement that it is permissible that the definer is the whole as a whole, not any of its parts, that is the true answer, which will come in the subsequent discussion in establishing the objection.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument from the Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) for the impossibility of acquiring conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) through acquisition (kasb). The argument is that if a conceptual essence (māhiyya) is to be known through acquisition, it must be defined either by itself, by its part, or by something external. All three are invalid. The first (definition by itself) leads to circularity because the definer must be known before the defined. The second (definition by all its parts) fails because the parts are the same as the thing. The third (definition by external) fails unless the external encompasses all individuals uniquely. The commentary then explains each point in detail, including grammatical and logical nuances. The discussion also addresses potential objections, such as the possibility that the defining part might be composite, and the conclusion that the correct answer is to define by the whole as a whole, which will be discussed later.

Key Terms

تصور
كسب
تعريف
جزء
خارج
بديهة
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining a conceptual essence by one of its parts is acceptable because the part is simpler.
Correction: The argument shows that such definition leads to either self-definition (circularity) or definition by something external (also problematic), as every part is external to the others.

Checkpoint
Why does the Imam reject defining the conceptual essence by itself?
A) Because it would require knowing the definer before the defined, which is circular
B) Because the essence is unknown
C) Because the definition would be too long
D) Because the part cannot define the whole

Key Takeaway
Conceptual essence cannot be defined by itself, its part, or external without leading to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand your faith deeply, do you rely more on rational definitions or on divine revelation? How does this lesson challenge your default approach?
?
Think of a concept in Islam you find difficult to grasp—like the nature of the soul or the attributes of Allah. Are you trying to define it by itself, by its parts, or by something external? What does this lesson suggest about the futility of that attempt?
?
This lesson reveals the limits of human reason in defining essences. Will you accept that some truths can only be known through submission and revelation, not through intellectual mastery? How will you adjust your heart in the face of this limitation?

Refuting the Claim that the Whole is Not Its Parts
The author presents and refutes the objection that the whole is not identical to its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the whole can have properties different from its parts, and why this undermines the objection to the identity of essence and its parts.

Core Idea
The whole is not merely the sum of its parts; it can have distinct properties.

This passage from the Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a key objection to the identity of essence and its parts, and a clever refutation using everyday examples. The author shows that the relationship between parts and whole is not simply transitive, and that the collective whole can have properties that no individual part possesses. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the kalam doctrine on the nature of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: an objection, a refutation, supporting arguments, and a final distinction.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that states the objection: 'If all the parts were not the essence itself, then either each part would be inside it, so it would not be all, or outside it, so it would not be parts.' Understand the dilemma being posed.
3
Examine the main refutation
The author responds by saying that the precedence of each part over the essence does not necessitate the precedence of the whole. He gives two examples: a house that can accommodate each person but not all together, and an army that defeats an enemy no single soldier can. These show that the whole can have different properties.
Think about why these examples work logically.
4
Understand the supporting argument
The author adds that if the objection were valid, the whole would be prior to itself, which is absurd. This strengthens the refutation by showing the objection leads to a contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum within the refutation.
5
Note the final distinction
The author distinguishes between material parts and formal parts. If the objector means all parts (material and formal), the refutation stands. If only material parts, then those are not truly 'all' but a subset, and defining the essence by them is incomplete.
This shows precision in theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : لو لم يكن جميع الأجزاء نفس الماهية فإما أن يكون داخلا فيها فلا يكون جميعاً ، أو خارجاً عنها فلا يكون أجزاء (و) قلنا : في دفعه بطريق المناقضة ( لا يلزم من تقدم كل من الأجزاء على الماهية ) تقدم الكل عليها ) فإن الكل المجموعي وكل واحد قد يتخالفان في الأحكام، فإن كل إنسان تسعه هذه الدار التي لا تسع كلهم، وكل العسكر يهزم العدو الذي لا يهزمه كل واحد منهم، بل نقول : كل واحد الأجزاء جزء من الكل المجموعي الذي ليس جزءاً لنفسه، ثم إنه أيد هذه المناقضة بقوله ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يصح ما ذكرناه من أنه ليس يلزم من م كل واحد على شيء تقدم الكل عليه ) تقدم الكل ) أي كل الأجزاء ) على نفسه ) لأن كل واحد منها متقدم على كلها كتقدمه على الماهية ،بعينه ويمكن أن يجعل هذا نقضاً إجمالياً كما لا يخفى فإن أراد هذا المجيب بجميع الأجزاء جميعها مطلقاً بحيث يتناول المادية والصورية معاً، فدفع جوابه ما قدمناه ( وإن أراد ) به ( الأجزاء المادية ) فقط (لم يكن ما أراده أعني الأجزاء المادية وحدها ( جميعاً) حقيقة بل بعضاً داخلاً في القسم الثاني ) ولا كافية في معرفة كنه الماهية ) فلا يكون التعريف بها حداً تاماً، والكلام فيه
The clearest expression is to say: If all the parts were not the essence itself, then either each part would be inside it, so it would not be 'all', or outside it, so it would not be 'parts'. And we said in refuting this by way of contradiction: The precedence of each part over the essence does not necessitate the precedence of the whole over it, for the collective whole and each individual may differ in properties: for example, a house can accommodate each individual but not all of them together, and an army can defeat an enemy that no single soldier can defeat. Rather we say: each part is a part of the collective whole, which is not a part of itself. Then he supported this contradiction by saying: 'Otherwise' – meaning if what we said is not valid, that the precedence of each part over something does not necessitate the precedence of the whole over it – then the whole (i.e. all the parts) would be prior to itself, because each part is prior to the whole just as it is prior to the essence. This can be taken as a general refutation, as is clear. If this respondent intends by 'all parts' the absolute totality including both material and formal parts, then his answer is refuted by what we have already said. But if he intends only the material parts, then what he intends (i.e. the material parts alone) is not truly 'all', but rather a part falling under the second division, and insufficient for knowing the essence in reality, so the definition by them would not be a complete definition. And the discussion about it.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its refutation concerning the relationship between the essence (al-mahiyyah) and its parts. The objection is: if all the parts are not the essence itself, then each part must be either inside the essence (so it is not 'all' but a part) or outside it (so it is not a part of it). The refutation uses the idea that the collective whole and each individual part can have different properties. The author gives two examples: a house can contain each person individually but not all together, and an army can defeat an enemy that no single soldier can. Thus, the precedence of each part over the essence does not entail the precedence of the whole over it. The author further supports this by arguing that if the objection's logic were valid, the whole would be prior to itself. Finally, the author distinguishes between material and formal parts: if the objector means all parts (material and formal), the answer is already refuted; if only material parts, then they are not truly 'all' but a subset, and defining the essence by them alone is insufficient for a complete definition.

Key Terms
الأجزاء
الماهية
الكل المجموعي
التقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if each part precedes the whole, then the whole must also precede itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that the collective whole can have properties different from its parts; thus, the precedence of parts does not entail precedence of the whole.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that the collective whole can have different properties from its individual parts?
A) A house can accommodate each person but not all together.
B) A tree is composed of roots, trunk, and branches.
C) Water is made of hydrogen and oxygen.
D) A number is the sum of its digits.

Key Takeaway
The whole can have properties that its parts lack, so the precedence of parts does not imply precedence of the whole, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you reduce something complex to its parts—like defining your identity by your achievements, roles, or body? How does this lesson warn you against neglecting the 'whole' that transcends the sum?
?
The whole can have properties that its parts lack. Consider your relationship with Allah: are you treating your acts of worship as isolated parts, or are you seeking the whole of taqwa that emerges beyond them? What must you change today?
?
You define yourself by your deeds, but your essence is more than your deeds. If you were to be defined only by your visible parts, would that definition be complete? What hidden part of your 'whole'—your sincerity, your love for Allah—is missing from how others see you, and how can you nurture it?

Al-Urmawi on Conception of Parts and Whole: The Mirror Analogy
Al-Urmawi argues that parts' images yield a single conception, but the commentator corrects him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conception of parts relates to the conception of the whole essence, and the commentator's correction using the mirror analogy.

Core Idea
The image of the whole is the ordered images of its parts, not a separate mental entity.

This passage presents an important debate in Islamic epistemology about how we conceive composite essences. Al-Qadi al-Urmawi suggests that conceiving each part separately leads to a new conception of the whole, but the commentator argues that the combined images themselves constitute the conception of the whole, using a mirror analogy. This lays groundwork for understanding the unity of knowledge and the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get an overview of the argument.
Notice the dense style with many parentheses.
2
Identify al-Urmawi's claim and the objection
Al-Urmawi claims that conceptions of parts lead to a single conception of the whole. The commentator objects that this implies a separate mental entity, which intuitive experience denies.
3
Understand the mirror analogy
The commentator compares each part's image to a mirror for that part. When combined and linked, they become a single mirror for the whole. This shows that the combined images are the conception of the whole, not a separate product.
The mirror analogy is central to the correction.
4
Grasp the unity of knowledge and known
The commentator notes that the conception of the essence is identical in essence to the conceptions of the parts, differing only in consideration. This is the principle of ittihad al-ilm wa al-malum.
This will be explained further in a later discussion of knowledge.
5
Reflect on the significance
This lesson shows how defining essences involves the parts' conceptions, not a separate mental entity. It clarifies the methodology of conceptual analysis in kalam.
Consider how this applies to definitions of complex entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال غيره : ( وهو القاضي الأرموي ( بجميع تصورات الأجزاء يحصل تصور واحد لجميع الأجزاء) ومحصله على ما لخصه في بعض كتبه أن وإن كان نفس الماهية بالذات إلا أنهما يتغايران بالاعتبار فإنه قد يتعلق بكل جزء تصور على حدة، فيكون هناك تصورات بعدد الأجزاء وقد يتعلق تصور واحد بجميع الأجزاء، فمجموع التصورات المتعلقة بالأجزاء تفصيلا هو المعرف الموصل إلى التصور الواحد المتعلق الأجزاء إجمالاً، وليس في ذلك تقدم شيء على نفسه، ولا بجميع شك أن المتبادر من هذه العبارة هو أنا إذا تصورنا كل واحد من الأجزاء حتى اجتمعت في ذهننا تصوراتها معاً مرتبة يحصل لنا حينئذ تصور آخر مغاير لذلك المجموع المرتب، متعلق بجميع الأجزاء هو تصور الماهية والوجدان يكذبه فلذلك قال ) والحق أن الأجزاء إذا استحضرت ) في الذهن ( مرتبة) مقيداً بعضها ببعض (حتى حصلت صورها فيه مجتمعة (فهي) فتلك الأجزاء المستحضرة المرتبة ( الماهية ) يعني أن تلك الصور المجتمعة تصور الماهية بالكنه بل عينها كما ستعرفه ( لا أن ثمة مجموعاً) من التصورات ( يوجب ) ذلك المجموع ( حصول شيء آخر) في الذهن ( هو الماهية ) أي تصورها وتوضيحه أن صورة كل جزء مرآة يشاهد بها ذلك الجزء قصداً، فإذا اجتمعت صورتان وتقيدت إحداهما بالأخرى صارتا معاً مرآة واحدة يشاهد بها مجموع الجزءين قصداً، ويشاهد بها كل واحد منهما ضمناً، وهذا هو تصور الماهية بالكنه الحاصل بالاكتساب من تصوري الجزءين، ومتحد معهما بالذات ومغاير لهما بالاعتبار على قياس حال الماهية بالنسبة إلى جميع أجزائها ( فالمعرف ( للماهية ) مجموع أمور كل واحد منهما متقدم على الماهية وله مدخل صرفه الشارح إلى ما هو المقصود من أن الأجزاء المستحضرة من حيث إنها مستحضرة هي الصور، وإن قوله : فهي الماهية على حذف المضاف أي تصورها . قوله : (بل عينها أي تصور الماهية عين الماهية بالذات ويمكن أن يعبر عنه بالماهية فلا حاجة إلى حذف المضاف غاية ما في الباب أن يراد من حيث قيامها بالذهن. قوله : ( كما ستعرفه أي في بحث العلم من أن العلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات، مختلفان بالاعتبار، فمن حيث القيام بالذهن يسمى علما، ومع قطع النظر عنه يسمى معلوماً . قوله : ( والكلام فيه لأن الجواب على اختيار الشق الأول من الشق الثاني، هو أن التعريف الأجزاء والتعريف به حد تام. قوله : (يعني أن تلك الصور المجتمعة (الخ لما كان ظاهر كلام المصنف يفيد القدح في كون مجموع الأجزاء أمراً يوجب حصولها حصول أمر آخر هو الماهية، وليس المتبادر من كلام الأرموي ذلك بل أن يكون تصورات الأجزاء أمراً يوجب حصولها حصول أمر آخر مغاير لها بالذات، هو تصور المجموع أعني الماهية وجه الشارح كلام المصنف بحيث أفاد القدح في ذلك المتبادر ، حيث قال: يعني أن تلك الصور الخ وبين المجموع بقوله : من التصورات والماهية بقوله أي تصورها والمقصود بذلك دفع اعتراض شارح المقاصد، وأشار بقوله : بل عينها إلى أن المقصود الأصلي ها هنا وإن كان تصور الماهية إلا أنه عبر عنه بالماهية تنبيهاً على اتحاد العلم والمعلوم .
And another said (namely al-Qadi al-Urmawi): 'Through all the conceptions of the parts, a single conception of all the parts is obtained.' His summary, as he summarized in some of his books, is that although the essence is identical in itself, they differ in consideration: for a conception may be attached to each part separately, resulting in conceptions equal to the number of parts, and a single conception may be attached to all the parts. So the totality of the conceptions of the parts in detail is the cognitive means leading to the single conception that relates to the parts as a whole, and there is no precedence of anything over itself in that. But there is no doubt that what is understood from this expression is that when we conceive each part until their conceptions are gathered in our mind together in order, then another conception arises, different from that ordered totality, relating to all the parts, which is the conception of the essence. But intuitive experience denies this. Therefore he (the commentator) said: 'But the truth is that when the parts are present in the mind in order, linked to one another, until their images are collected in it, then those present ordered parts are the essence.' That is, those collected images are the conception of the essence in depth, indeed its very self, as you will later know – not that there is a totality of conceptions that necessitates the occurrence of something else in the mind which is the essence, i.e. its conception. And its clarification is that the image of each part is a mirror by which that part is seen intentionally; when two images are combined and one is linked to the other, they together become a single mirror by which the sum of the two parts is seen intentionally, and each of them is seen implicitly. This is the conception of the essence in depth, acquired from the conceptions of the two parts, united with them in essence and different from them in consideration, analogously to the state of the essence relative to all its parts. So the cognitive means to the essence is a totality of things each of which is prior to the essence and has an entry. The commentator redirected this to what is intended: that the parts present, insofar as they are present, are the images, and his statement 'they are the essence' is with the deletion of the annex, i.e. their conception. His statement (rather its very self) means the conception of the essence is the essence itself in essence, and it is possible to express it as the essence, so there is no need to delete the annex; the only point is that it is considered insofar as it subsists in the mind. His statement (as you will know) means in the discussion of knowledge: that knowledge and the known are united in essence, different in consideration; in terms of subsistence in the mind it is called knowledge, and disregarding that it is called known. His statement (and the discussion about it) because the answer, on choosing the first alternative from the second, is that the definition by parts and the definition with it is a complete definition. His statement (meaning that those collected images etc.) – since the apparent meaning of the author's words implies a criticism of the idea that the totality of parts necessitates the occurrence of another thing which is the essence, but what is understood from al-Urmawi's words is not that, but rather that the conceptions of the parts necessitate the occurrence of another thing different from them in essence, which is the conception of the totality, i.e. the essence. The commentator redirected the author's words to imply criticism of that common understanding, where he said: 'meaning that those images etc.' and he explained 'the totality' by his words 'of conceptions' and 'the essence' by his words 'its conception'. The purpose of that is to rebut the objection of the Sharh al-Maqasid. And he indicated by his words 'rather its very self' that the original intent here, although it is the conception of the essence, he expressed it as 'the essence' to alert to the unity of knowledge and the known.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents al-Qadi al-Urmawi's view that the conception of the parts in detail leads to a single conception of the whole (the essence). The commentator then corrects this by clarifying that the mental images of the parts themselves, when present in the mind in an ordered manner, constitute the conception of the essence – not that they produce a separate additional entity. The commentator uses the analogy of a mirror: each part's image is like a mirror for that part; when combined, they become a single mirror for the whole. The conception of the essence is thus identical in essence to the conceptions of the parts, differing only in consideration. This is linked to the broader principle of the unity of knowledge and the known (ittihad al-ilm wa al-malum), which the commentator says will be explained later. Finally, the commentator notes that al-Urmawi's words, when properly understood, do not imply a separate mental entity, and the critic from Sharh al-Maqasid is thus rebutted.

مرآة
اتحاد العلم والمعلوم
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceiving the parts in detail produces a new, separate mental concept of the whole.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the ordered images of the parts are themselves the conception of the whole; no separate entity arises.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the commentator use to explain how the parts' images combine to form the conception of the whole?

B) A mirror for each part that becomes a single mirror when combined.
C) A chain made of links.

Key Takeaway
The conception of a composite essence is nothing other than the ordered images of its parts, united by consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The mirror analogy shows that the whole is seen through the ordered parts. In your daily life, are your actions (parts) arranged in a way that collectively reflects your true identity as a Muslim? If not, what one part needs reordering to restore the clarity of the whole?
?
Al-Urmawi's mistake was to imagine a separate 'spiritual experience' beyond the actual practices. What would it mean to accept that your connection with Him is precisely your ordered, sincere acts—nothing more, nothing less?

The Mental Representation of Parts Is the Quiddity Itself
Sanusi argues that mental representation of ordered parts is identical to the quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the mental representation of parts in order is identical to the quiddity, not a separate representation.

Core Idea
The mind's ordered picture of the parts is the quiddity itself.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a subtle point in metaphysics: when we conceive of a whole by its parts, is the resulting concept an additional mental entity or simply the ordered parts themselves? Sanusi argues it is the latter, using the powerful analogy of mirrors merging into one. Understanding this resolves confusion about how definitions work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before analyzing.
Notice that the passage opens with a statement of the correct view.
2
Identify the denial
The author explicitly denies that the totality of part-conceptualizations produces a separate entity that is the quiddity. Find that denial.
3
Examine the mirror analogy
The author uses a mirror analogy: each part's form is a mirror; combined mirrors become one mirror showing the whole intentionally and parts implicitly. Understand this step.
This illustrates how the mental representation is not additive but unified.
4
Connect to external parts
The author analogizes the mental representation to external parts constituting the quiddity. Recognize that just as external parts together are the quiddity, so too mental parts are the quiddity.
This reinforces that the mental representation is not an extra layer but the very essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن الأجزاء إذا استحضرت في الذهن مرتبة مقيداً بعضها ببعض حتى حصلت صورها فيه مجتمعة فهي تلك الأجزاء المستحضرة المرتبة الماهية يعني أن تلك الصور المجتمعة تصور الماهية بالكنه بل عينها كما ستعرفه لا أن ثمة مجموعاً من التصورات يوجب ذلك المجموع حصول شيء آخر في الذهن هو الماهية أي تصورها وتوضيحه أن صورة كل جزء مرآة يشاهد بها ذلك الجزء قصداً، فإذا اجتمعت صورتان وتقيدت إحداهما بالأخرى صارتا معاً مرآة واحدة يشاهد بها مجموع الجزءين قصداً، ويشاهد بها كل واحد منهما ضمناً، وهذا هو تصور الماهية بالكنه الحاصل بالاكتساب من تصوري الجزءين، ومتحد معهما بالذات ومغاير لهما بالاعتبار على قياس حال الماهية بالنسبة إلى جميع أجزائها فالمعرف للماهية مجموع أمور كل واحد منهما متقدم على الماهية وله مدخل في تعريفها، وأما المجموع المركب منها الحاصل في الذهن فهو تصور الماهية المطلوب بالاكتساب الذي هو جميع تلك الأمور وترتيبها وما أحسن ما قيل: حدست تصورات مجموع تصورات محدود وهذا المجموع وتعريفه للماهية في الذهن كالأجزاء الخارجية وتقويمها للماهية في الخارج فإنها متقومة بجميع الأجزاء بمعنى أنه ما من جزء من الأجزاء الخارجة إلا وله مدخل في التقويم والكل أي جميع الأجزاء مجتمعاً هو الماهية بعينها لا أنها تترتب عليه أي على جميع الأجزاء فكما أن جميع الأجزاء الخارجية المجتمعة عين الماهية واجتماعها فيه ليس جزءاً منها بل خارج عنها لازم لها، كذلك جميع الأجزاء في الذهن عين الماهية، واجتماعها فيه أمر خارج عنها لازم لها وكما أن كل واحد من الأجزاء الخارجية مقوم للماهية متقدم عليها في الخارج كذلك كل واحد من الأجزاء الذهنية مقوم لها
The truth is that when the parts are brought to mind in order, bound one to another, until their forms are present in it together, then those present ordered parts are the quiddity, meaning that those gathered forms are a conceptualization of the quiddity in its essence, rather it is its very substance, as you will know, not that there is a totality of conceptualizations that necessitates that totality bringing about another thing in the mind which is the quiddity, that is, its conceptualization. Its clarification is that the form of each part is a mirror through which that part is seen intentionally; when two forms come together and one is bound to the other, they together become one mirror through which the sum of the two parts is seen intentionally, and through it each one of them is seen implicitly. This is the conceptualization of the quiddity in its essence obtained by acquisition from the conceptualizations of the two parts, united with them in essence and different from them by consideration, analogously to the state of the quiddity with respect to all its parts. So the definer of the quiddity is a totality of things, each of which is prior to the quiddity and has an entry in its definition. As for the composite totality resulting from them present in the mind, it is the conceptualization of the quiddity sought by acquisition, which is all those things and their arrangement. How excellent is what was said: 'You guessed conceptualizations; total conceptualizations of a limited thing.' And this totality and its definition of the quiddity in the mind is like the external parts and their constituting (taqwīm) of the quiddity externally, for it is constituted by all the parts, meaning that there is no part of the external parts except that it has an entry in the constitution. The whole, that is, all the parts together, is the quiddity itself, not that it is arranged from them, that is, from all the parts. So just as all the external parts together are the very quiddity, and their togetherness in it is not a part of it but rather external to it and necessary for it, likewise all the parts in the mind are the very quiddity, and their togetherness in it is a matter external to it and necessary for it. And just as each of the external parts is constitutive (muqawwim) of the quiddity prior to it externally, so too each of the mental parts is constitutive of it.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the relationship between the mental representation of parts and the quiddity. The author begins by stating the correct view: when the parts are brought to mind in an ordered and mutually bound way, the resulting mental forms are not a separate representation of the quiddity but rather the quiddity itself in its essence. He clarifies that it is not that the totality of part-representations generates a new distinct representation; rather, the gathered forms are exactly the conceptualization of the quiddity. To illustrate, he uses the mirror analogy: each part's form is a mirror showing that part intentionally. When two such mirrors are combined and bound, they become one mirror showing the sum intentionally and each part implicitly. This combined view is the conceptualization of the quiddity by acquisition, identical in essence to the part-conceptualizations yet considered distinct by rational consideration. He then extends the analogy to the whole definition: just as external parts constitute the external quiddity (the whole is the quiddity itself, not something built from parts), so too in the mind, the collection of mental parts is the very quiddity, and their togetherness is an external necessary concomitant, not a component. Each mental part is constitutive and prior, just as each external part is constitutive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mental representation of the whole is a separate additional representation over and above the representations of the parts.
Correction: The author stresses it is identical to the parts in a certain order, not an extra entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the mental representation of the parts (when ordered and bound) and the quiddity?
A) The representation of the parts produces a separate mental entity that is the quiddity.
B) The representation of the parts is itself the quiddity, not a separate entity.
C) The quiddity is prior to the parts and independent of them.
D) The parts and the quiddity are unrelated in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The mental representation of parts in proper order is not a stepping stone to the quiddity; it is the quiddity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own understanding of 'iman', do you reduce it to a list of beliefs and actions, or do you see its mental representation as the very reality of iman? How does this lesson challenge you to unify your conceptual knowledge into a single, living reality?
?
The author insists that the mental representation of parts bound together is the quiddity itself. Where in your religious knowledge do you separate the parts from the whole, failing to see them as one? What would it mean for your worship if you truly believed your mental picture of Allah's attributes is not a shadow but the very essence you seek?
?
Sanusi's mirror analogy says each part is seen intentionally, but the whole is seen implicitly. In your daily life, are you focused on 'intentional parts' (specific acts) while neglecting the implicit whole (sincere devotion)? How can you adjust your mental representation to make the implicit explicit?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Understanding the Second Fallacy: Confusing All Parts with the Whole
Defining the mental composite and exposing the fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core mistake of confusing the aggregate of all parts with the whole essence in definition, and how this fallacy is used to argue against the composition of existence.

Core Idea
The second fallacy equates 'all parts' with the whole, missing that the whole is the essence itself, not an aggregate.

We now turn to the second fallacy concerning the distinction between the parts and the whole in definition. The author clarifies that the sum of parts in the mind is identical to the essence conceived, but the aggregation of those parts is external to the essence. The error lies in conflating this aggregate with the whole essence. This lesson explains the distinction and shows how al-Imam al-Razi applies the fallacy to deny composition in existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mental composite
Read the opening lines about the definition of the composite in the mind and the analogy with external parts. Notice how the author distinguishes between 'all parts' and 'the whole'.
Pay attention to the parallelism between external and mental parts.
2
Identify the core distinction
The passage says: just as the aggregate of external parts is identical to the external essence, and their aggregation is external to it, similarly all mental parts are identical to the mental essence, with aggregation external. The error is confusing the aggregate (all parts) with the whole (essence).
3
See al-Razi's application
Read how al-Imam al-Razi applies this fallacy to deny external composition of existence: if parts are existences, each part equals the whole; if non-existences, then either existence would be non-existence or the additional factor is existence. This is the fallacy at work.
The proof would be sound without the fallacy.
4
Reflect on the basis
The passage ends with the basis of the fallacy: the idea that parts produce the whole and the whole is arranged upon what produces it. Consider why this leads to confusion.
