في تعريفها، وأما المجموع المركب منها الحاصل في الذهن فهو تصور الماهية المطلوب بالاكتساب الذي هو جميع تلك الأمور وترتيبها وما أحسن ما قيل: حدست تصورات مجموع تصورات محدود ( وهذا المجموع وتعريفه للماهية في الذهن ( كالأجزاء الخارجية وتقويمها للماهية) في الخارج ( فإنها متقومة بجميع أنه ما الأجزاء بمعنى من جزء من الأجزاء الخارجة ( إِلا وله مدخل في التقويم والكل ) أي على جميع الأجزاء مجتمعاً ( هو الماهية ) بعينها ( لا أنها تترتب عليه ) أي جميع الأجزاء فكما أن جميع الأجزاء الخارجية المجتمعة عين الماهية واجتماعها فيه ليس جزءاً منها بل خارج عنها لازم لها، كذلك جميع الأجزاء في الذهن عين الماهية، واجتماعها فيه أمر خارج عنها لازم لها وكما أن كل واحد من الأجزاء الخارجية مقوم للماهية متقدم عليها في الخارج كذلك كل واحد من الأجزاء الذهنية مقوم لها
قوله : ( هذه المغلطة الثانية الخ فيه بحث أما أولاً فلان إيراد هذه المقدمة في أثناء الجواب عن الشبهة لا وجه له حينئذ وأما ثانياً فلان ما نقله الشارح في الوجود مما لا مساس له بهذه الشبهة بحسب الظاهر فكان على الشارح أن يبين أن ما ذكره في الوجود مرجعه هذه الشبهة، وإن وقع فيها تغيير ما ، وأما ثالثاً فلانه على هذا التقدير لا فائدة في قوله وستراه الخ، وما توهم من أنه نقض لشبهة الإمام فليس بشيء لأنه لا دخل لطرد الإمام في كونه نقضاً، ولأنه إنما يكون إشارة إلى النقض لو قال : بطرد هذه الشبهة بعينها في نفي التركيب الخارجي لأنه بالتغيير لا تبقى تلك الشبهة فيجوز أن يكون استلزامها لنفي التركيب بواسطة ذلك التغيير، وعندي في حل هذه العبارة أن قوله : هذه المغلطة إشارة إلى الغلط في الفرق بين جميع الأجزاء والكل الذي الماهية، ولذا عبر عنه بالمغلطة وفائدة قوله : وستراه الخ الإشارة إلى أن ما ذكرناه من تحقيق الفرق يدفع ما أورده في نفي التركيب عن الوجود وغيره فلا يفيد مرة ثانية بل يكتفي فيه عن الجواب إجمالاً وبيان طرد هذه المغلطة في نفس التركيب عن الوجود أن قوله : كان الوجود محض ما ليس بوجود إنما يتم لو كان جميع الأجزاء التي فرضت غير وجودات عين الوجود، الذي هو مجموع الأجزاء، وإلا فاللازم أن يكون جميع أجزاء الوجود محض ما ليس بوجود، وكذا قوله : فذلك الزائد هو الوجود مبني على أن تلك الأجزاء نفس المجموع للأجزاء، وهي ليست الوجود فيكون الزائد هو الوجود فتدبر والله الموفق .
قوله : فكما أن جميع الأجزاء إلى قوله أمر خارج عنها لازم (لها فيه بحث لأن الاجتماع لما كان خارجاً عن الماهية لازماً لها، تكون الماهية مجموع الجنس والفصل فقط، فإذا كانا حاصلين تكون الماهية حاصلة من غير أثر للنظر والاكتساب، إذ لا شك في حصول الشيء عن حصول جميع أجزائه، فما معنى أجزائه، فما معنى ما سيذكره في تحقيق الاكتساب من أن هذا المجموع إنما يحصل بالكسب الذي هو جميع تلك الأجزاء وترتيبها .
متقدم عليها في الذهن ولما كان جواب القاضي محتملاً لهذا المعنى أيضاً لم يرد عليه جزماً، بل أشار بقوله : والحق إلى إشعاره بما ليس حقاً (وستراه) أي الإمام الرازي ( يطرد هذه المغلطة الثانية ( في التركيب الخارجي عن بعض الأشياء بتغيير ) فيقول : في نفس التركيب عن الوجود مثلا إن كانت أجزاؤه وجودات ساوى الجزء كله في تمام الماهية ، وإن كانت غير وجودات، فإن لم يحصل عند اجتماعها أمر زائد كان الوجود محض ما ليس بوجود وإن حصل فذلك الزائد هو الوجود، وتلك الأمور معروضات لا أجزاؤه وأنت خبير بأن هذا لو تم لدل على انتفاء التركيب عن الوجود مطلقاً، سواء كان تركيباً خارجياً أو ذهنياً، فالأولى أن لا يقيد التركيب بالخارجي إلا أنه قيده به إشعاراً بأن هذه المغلطة سفسطة، لاستلزامها انتفاء التركيب الخارجي
قوله : ( إن كانت أجزاؤه وجودات) أي ما يصدق عليه الوجود صدق الذاتي سواء كان تمام ماهيتها، فتكون تلك الأجزاء مختلفة بالعدد أو داخلاً فيها، فتكون تلك الأجزاء مختلفة بالنوع . قوله : ( ساوى الجزء كله في تمام (الماهية أي الجزء بلا واسطة إن كانت مختلفة بالعدد، وبواسطة إن كانت مختلفة بالنوع، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجزء جزءاً ولا الكل كلاً، ويلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه كما في المباحث المشرقية .
قوله : ( وإن كانت غير وجودات) أي لم يصدق عليها صدق الذاتي .
قوله : ( أمر زائد) أي عارض كما يدل عليه قوله معروضاته .
قوله : ( لا أجزاؤه ) وقد فرض أنها أجزاؤه هذا ،خلف وبما حررنا لك ظهر أن الدليل المذكور تام على نفي التركيب لولا عدم الفرق بين جميع الأجزاء والكل . قوله : ( وأنت خبير الخ فيه بحث لأن دلالته على نفي التركيب لا ينافي ما ذكره المصنف من طرد الإمام إياه في نفي التركيب الخارجي حيث قال : الوجود بسيط فلا يحد فإن مقصوده أنه بسيط في نفسه، فالمراد بالتركيب الخارجي التركيب الخارج عن الذهن لا التركيب في الأعيان إذ لا سترة في عدم كون الوجود مركباً في الأعيان . قوله : ( إشعاراً) الخ فيه بحث أما أولاً فلان الإشعار المذكور خفي غاية الخفاء، وأما ثانياً
قوله : ( وستراه الخ قيل : فائدة هذا الكلام هي النقض الإجمالي على المتمسك الثاني بأنه لو صح بجميع مقدماته لما تخلف الحكم ولكن الحس شاهد بالتخلف فليس بصحيح ففيه أن النقض إنما يصح لو لم يغير الشبهة لجواز أن يكون الخلل عارضاً بعد التغيير، فينتقض المغير لا الأصل على أن التغيير في حد كأنه أخرج عن الأصل بالكلية فقوله : لتغيير ما ليس كما ينبغي إلا أنه لما أراد ترويج النقض ناسب له أن يقول ذلك، واعلم أن مبنى المغلطة الثانية وهو الطرد هو أن يقال : إن الأجزاء تحصل الكل والكل مرتب على ما يحصله ذهناً، كان كما في التعريف أو خارجاً فتأمل .
Concerning its definition: as for the composite sum obtained in the mind, it is the conception of the essence (māhiyya) sought by acquisition (iktisāb), which is all those matters and their arrangement. How excellent is what was said: I conjectured that the conceptions of a limited sum of conceptions (and this sum and its definition of the essence in the mind) are like the external parts and their constituting (taqwīm) of the essence) in the external world (for they are constituted by all that the parts mean, i.e., every part of the external parts, except that it has an entry in the constitution, and the whole) i.e., all the parts collectively, (is the essence) itself, (not that it is arranged upon them). i.e., all the parts: just as all the external parts collectively are identical to the essence, and their aggregation in it is not a part of it but is external to it and necessary for it, similarly all the parts in the mind are identical to the essence, and their aggregation in it is something external to it and necessary for it. And just as each one of the external parts is a constituent (muqawwim) of the essence, prior to it in the external world, similarly each one of the mental parts is a constituent of it. His statement: (This second fallacy etc.) contains discussion. Firstly, because the introduction of this premise during the response to the doubt (shubha) has no justification then. Secondly, because what the commentator (al-shāriḥ) transmitted concerning existence (wujūd) has no connection to this doubt in appearance, so it was incumbent on the commentator to clarify that what he mentioned concerning existence has its reference to this doubt, even if some alteration occurred in it. Thirdly, because on this estimation there is no benefit in his statement 'and you will see it' etc. And what is imagined that it is a refutation (naqḍ) of the doubt of al-Imām is nothing, because the sweeping application (ṭard) of al-Imām has no bearing on its being a refutation, and because it would only be an indication of refutation if he said: by sweeping application of this very doubt in denying external composition, because with the alteration that doubt does not remain, so it is possible that its entailment of denying composition is through that alteration. In my opinion in resolving this expression: his statement 'this fallacy' is an indication of the error in the distinction between all the parts and the whole which is the essence, and therefore he expressed it as 'fallacy'. And the benefit of his statement 'and you will see it' etc. is an indication that what we mentioned of the verification of the distinction repels what he presented in denying composition of existence and other things, so it does not benefit a second time, but suffices in it for an answer in summary, and an exposition of the sweeping application of this fallacy to composition itself concerning existence: that his statement 'existence would be pure non-existence' is only complete if all the parts that are assumed to be non-existences are identical to existence, which is the sum of the parts; otherwise, it is necessary that all parts of existence be pure non-existence. And likewise his statement: 'that surplus is existence' is based on those parts being identical to the sum of the parts, and they are not existence, so the surplus is existence. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. His statement: 'Just as all the parts up to his statement something external to it necessary for it' contains discussion: because since aggregation is external to the essence, necessary for it, the essence becomes only the sum of genus and differentia; so if they are obtained, the essence is obtained without any effect of consideration and acquisition, since there is no doubt that a thing is obtained when all its parts are obtained. So what is the meaning of 'its parts'? So what is the meaning of what he will mention in the verification of acquisition, that this sum is obtained only by the acquisition which is all those parts and their arrangement? Prior to them in the mind. And since the answer of al-Qāḍī was also possible for this meaning, he did not decisively reject it, but indicated by his statement 'and the truth' to suggest what is not true. (And you will see him) i.e., al-Imām al-Rāzī, (apply this second fallacy) to external composition (tarkīb khārijī) concerning some things with an alteration, saying: concerning composition itself of existence, for example: if its parts are existences, then the part equals the whole in the complete essence; and if they are non-existences, then if upon their coming together no additional thing occurs, existence would be pure non-existence; and if it occurs, then that additional thing is existence, and those things are accidents, not its parts. And you are aware that if this were complete, it would indicate the negation of composition in existence absolutely, whether it is external composition or mental composition. So it is better not to restrict composition to external, except that he restricted it to indicate that this fallacy is sophistry, because it entails the negation of external composition. His statement: (If its parts are existences) i.e., what is predicated of existence with essential predication, whether it is the entire quiddity, so those parts differ in number, or enters into it, so those parts differ in type. His statement: (Equals the part whole in the complete essence) i.e., the part without mediation if they differ in number, and with mediation if they differ in type; then it is necessary that the part not be a part and the whole not be a whole, and it is necessary that a thing precede itself, as in the Eastern investigations. His statement: (And if they are non-existences) i.e., they are not predicated with essential predication. His statement: (An additional thing) i.e., an accident, as indicated by his statement 'accidents'. His statement: (Not its parts) and it was assumed that they are its parts; this is a contradiction. And by what we have clarified to you, it appears that the mentioned proof is complete for denying composition were it not for the lack of distinction between all the parts and the whole. His statement: (And you are aware etc.) contains discussion: because its indication of denying composition does not contradict what the author mentioned of al-Imām's application of it in denying external composition, where he said: 'Existence is simple, so it cannot be defined', for his intent is that it is simple in itself. So what is meant by external composition is composition external to the mind, not composition in re (aʿyān), since there is no concealment in existence not being composite in re. His statement: (Indication etc.) contains discussion: firstly, because the mentioned indication is extremely hidden; secondly... His statement: (And you will see it etc.) It is said: the benefit of this speech is the summary refutation of the second adherent, that if it were sound with all its premises, the judgment would not be absent, but sense witnesses absence, so it is not sound. In it: the refutation is only sound if he did not alter the doubt, for it is possible that the defect occurs after the alteration, so the altered is refuted, not the original. Besides, the alteration in definition is such that it takes it out of the original entirely. So his statement 'with an alteration' is not as it should be, except that since he intended to promote the refutation, it was appropriate for him to say that. And know that the basis of the second fallacy, which is the sweeping application, is to say: The parts produce the whole, and the whole is arranged upon what produces it, either mentally as in the definition or externally. So reflect.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary examining the second fallacy (al-mughlaṭa al-thāniya) concerning the distinction between 'all the parts' (jamīʿ al-ajzāʾ) and 'the whole' (al-kull) which is the essence (al-māhiyya) in definition. It begins by clarifying the definition of the composite in the mind: the mental conception of the essence obtained through acquisition is the sum of all its parts and their arrangement. The author draws an analogy with external parts: just as the aggregate of external parts is identical to the external essence, and their aggregation is external to the essence but necessary for it, similarly all mental parts together are identical to the mental essence, with their aggregation being external and necessary. Each external part is a constituent (muqawwim) of the essence, and similarly each mental part is a constituent. Then the text discusses a statement about 'this second fallacy' and provides three critiques regarding its introduction in response to a doubt (shubha). It explains that the error lies in confusing 'all the parts' with 'the whole' (the essence). The passage then shows how al-Imām al-Rāzī applies this fallacy to deny external composition (al-tarkīb al-khārijī) in existence (al-wujūd) with an alteration. The argument is: if the parts of existence are existences, then each part equals the whole in essence, destroying the part-whole distinction; if the parts are non-existences, then either existence would be pure non-existence or the additional factor is existence itself, not the parts. The commentary concludes that this proof would deny composition absolutely if not for the failure to distinguish between 'all the parts' and 'the whole'. The author then examines various sub-arguments, noting that al-Rāzī's restriction to external composition indicates the fallacy is sophistry, and discusses criticisms and defenses of the refutation. The passage ends by stating the basis of the fallacy: the idea that parts produce the whole and the whole is arranged upon what produces it, whether mentally or externally.

Key Terms
الماهية (māhiyya)
الأجزاء (ajzāʾ)
الكل (kull)
التقويم (taqwīm)
التركيب الخارجي (tarkīb khārijī)
التركيب الذهني (tarkīb dhihnī)
الوجود (wujūd)
المغلطة (mughlaṭa)
الشبهة (shubha)
النقض (naqḍ)
الطرد (ṭard)
الكسب (kash/iktisāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'all the parts' and 'the whole' are the same thing in definition, leading to the false conclusion that composition is impossible.
Correction: The whole (essence) is identical to the aggregate of parts, but the aggregation itself is external to the essence; the fallacy fails to distinguish between the two.

Checkpoint
What is the core error identified in the 'second fallacy' according to this passage?
A) Denying that the essence is composed of parts
B) Confusing 'all the parts' with 'the whole' (the essence)
C) Assuming mental composition is identical to external composition
D) Rejecting the role of acquisition in definition

Key Takeaway
The second fallacy is the failure to distinguish between the aggregate of all parts and the whole (essence) itself; this error underlies false arguments against composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own understanding of God's attributes (ṣifāt), have you ever unconsciously treated them as separate 'parts' that add up to God? How does this lesson's distinction between the aggregate and the essence challenge that mental habit?
?
In your intellectual life, are you prone to reducing complex realities (like a person, a text, or a theological doctrine) to a sum of their parts? What would change if you truly saw the whole as the essence itself, not the aggregate?
?
This passage reveals a subtle fallacy that even great scholars can commit. Where in your own reasoning—especially in matters of faith—might you be confusing the collection of evidence or arguments with the truth itself? Will you commit to examining your assumptions before building arguments?

Defending Definition by Some Parts
Commentary refutes the objection that defining an essence by some parts leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining a quiddity by some of its parts, especially the formal part, does not lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
The formal part causes the whole without causing itself—circularity avoided.

In this passage, the commentator defends the permissibility of defining a quiddity using only some of its parts, countering an objection that such definition would lead to circularity. He introduces the key insight that the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) acts as a cause for the quiddity without being a cause for any of its individual parts, thereby breaking the cycle. This distinction resolves the apparent problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that defining by some parts requires knowing that part; if it is the same, circularity; if another, infinite regress. Read the passage to see how the commentary sets this up.
The objection is presented as 'it cannot be said...'
2
Learn the solution: the formal part as cause
The commentator argues that the formal part is a cause for the quiddity in external existence, but not for any of its own parts. Thus, it can serve as a definer without self-reference.
The key Arabic term is al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī (formal part).
3
Apply the analogy to mental existence
The same reasoning is extended to mental composites: mental existence is analogous to external, so the formal part can define the quiddity mentally as well.
This is implied in the passage when it discusses 'mental composite.'
4
Recognize the broader principle
The commentator concludes that the objection's premise—that one must know all parts—is false; knowing a distinctive aspect suffices.

، قوله : (لغيره) باللام الجارة لأن الكلام في تعريف الجزء لشيء من أجزاء الماهية الذي هو خارج، فضمير هو راجع إلى البعض الذي رجع إليه الضمير في تعريفه وضمير عنه إلى غيره ليكون التعريف بالخارج.

قوله : ( ألا يرى أن الجزء الصوري الخ قيل : أراد به ما هو بمنزلة الجزء الصوري من الجمع والانضمام اللازم له لا حقيقة كما هو المشهور فلا ينافي ما ذكره قبيل هذا من أن الاجتماع خارج عن المركب الخارجي، وما صرح به في أول الموقف الرابع من أن الهيئة الاجتماعية خارجة عن حقيقة الجسم لازمة لها، ففيه أن حديث العلية حينئذ لا يكاد يصح، وقيل: هذا على المشهور وما ذكره في الموضعين على التحقيق.
With the testimony of intuition (badīha) to the composition of some things externally: (this) i.e., as we mentioned, or we choose that (i.e., defining the quiddity (māhiyya)) by some parts, and that part may be (independent of definition) if its concept is necessary (ḍarūrī) or be (known by another) if its concept is theoretical (naẓarī). In both cases, defining the quiddity by it does not entail defining itself. So what was mentioned—that the knower of the quiddity must know all its parts—is absolutely false. It cannot be said: 'It is necessary to know something of its parts, and that is either itself or another, so one of the two absurdities follows as before' because we say: The knower of the quiddity must achieve knowledge of it in some manner that distinguishes it from others, and it is not entailed from achieving knowledge of something of its parts—do you not see that the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) is a cause (ʿilla) for the attainment of the quiddity in the external, while it is not a cause for the attainment of any of its parts in it? And whoever committed to what you mentioned chooses its definition by that, because then avoiding restriction to the external suggests that what he mentioned entails the negation of composition from composites absolutely. Thirdly, what is the benefit of the suggestion when he later explicitly stated that what he mentioned entails the negation of composition from composites known to be composite?
His statement: (It cannot be said... etc.) is another proof for the impossibility of definition by some parts.
His statement: (The formal part... etc.) means that the formal part in composites like the bed and the house is a cause for the attainment of the quiddity, since the formal is that by which a thing is in act (bi'l-fiʿl), and it is not a cause for the attainment of any of the parts of the composite—neither for the material part because it precedes the formal, nor for the formal part because of the impossibility of a thing being a cause of itself. And if the formal part is a cause for the attainment of the external composite while not being a cause for any of its parts, then let the like be permissible in the mental composite, for mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī) is analogous to external existence. And what he mentioned—that the conjunction (ijtimāʿ) of parts and the joining (inḍimām) of some with each other, like the joining of the formal with the material, is external to the quiddity—so there is no contradiction. So reflect, for feet have slipped in this. His statement: (What you mentioned) i.e., that the knower of the quiddity must know something of its parts. His statement: (by another) with the preposition lām, because the speech concerns defining a part for something of the parts of the quiddity which is external, so the pronoun 'he' refers to the part to which the pronoun in 'its definition' refers, and the pronoun 'it' to 'other' so that the definition is by external. His statement: (Or we choose that... etc.) It is not hidden that raising an objection to some premises of the mentioned proof is sufficient to rebut it, except that since they permitted definition by all parts, by some, and by external, one needs to investigate all the problems. His statement: (Do you not see that the formal part... etc.) It is said: He intended by it what is analogous to the formal part from the conjunction and joining necessary to it, not the reality (ḥaqīqa) as is well-known, so it does not contradict what he mentioned before this—that the conjunction is external to the external composite—and what he stated at the beginning of the fourth station (al-marqad al-rābiʿ)—that the configurational form (al-hayʾa al-ijtimāʿiyya) is external to the reality of the body and necessary to it. This contains that the talk of causality then cannot be correct. And it is said: This is according to the well-known, and what he mentioned in the two places is according to verification (taḥqīq).

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of defining a quiddity (māhiyya) by some of its parts, against an objection that doing so leads to circularity. The commentator begins by stating that with the testimony of intuition (badīha) that some things have external composition, one can either accept the previous argument or choose to define the quiddity by some parts. Those parts may be self-evident (ḍarūrī) or known by something else (naẓarī), but in neither case does defining the quiddity by them entail defining themselves. The objection that 'one must know something of its parts, which leads to either self-definition or infinite regress' is rejected because the knower of the quiddity needs only to obtain knowledge that distinguishes it, not to know all parts. The commentator then introduces the concept of the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) as a cause (ʿilla) for the quiddity in the external world, without being a cause for any of its individual parts (neither material nor formal). By analogy, the same is allowed in the mental composite. The objection that the conjunction of parts is external to the quiddity is noted, but the commentator warns that many have slipped on this point. The commentary also explains the grammatical construction of 'by another' (bi-ghayrihī) and clarifies that the allowance to define by all parts, some parts, or external parts requires a thorough investigation of all problems. Finally, a discussion notes that the formal part analogy may be interpreted as referring to the necessary conjunction rather than the true formal part, and this aligns with other statements about the configurational form (hayʾa ijtimāʿiyya) being external to the essence, though some hold the causal interpretation to be problematic.

هيئة اجتماعية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining a quiddity by a part always requires knowing that part itself, leading to circularity.
Correction: The commentator shows that the formal part can cause knowledge of the whole without being self-reflexive, because it is a cause for the quiddity but not for any of its own parts.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does defining a quiddity by its formal part not lead to circularity?

B) Because the formal part is a cause for the quiddity without being a cause for any of its own parts.
C) Because definition is only possible if all parts are known simultaneously.
D) Because the formal part is self-evident and needs no definition.

Key Takeaway
Defining a quiddity by its formal part is valid because the formal part serves as a cause for the quiddity without causing any of its own parts, thus avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you identify a truly self-evident starting point that does not need further justification?

Did you check whether that 'something else' was itself well-defined, or did you unknowingly fall into a vicious circle? Refuting Definition by Parts with the Formal Part
Refutation of definition-by-parts objection using formal part example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) resolves the objection that defining a whole by its parts leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
The formal part defines the whole without requiring prior knowledge of all parts.

This passage addresses a critical objection in the theory of definition. The opponent argues that if a quiddity is defined by some of its parts, those parts must themselves be known, leading to a vicious circle or infinite regress. The commentator refutes this by introducing the example of the formal part, which causes the existence of the composite without causing any of its own parts. This shows that a part can serve as a defining element without needing to define its constituents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line: 'It is not said: It is necessary that it know something of its parts...' Recognize the opponent's claim that defining by parts leads to a dilemma (circle or regress).
The objection is based on the idea that knowledge of a part requires knowledge of its parts, ad infinitum.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator counters that definition only requires a distinguishing mark, not exhaustive knowledge of all parts. The goal is to identify the quiddity, not to analyze every component.
3
Analyze the formal part example
The formal part (e.g., the shape of a bed) is the cause of the whole composite in external existence, but it is not the cause of any of its parts (neither the material part nor itself). Thus, it can define the whole without defining its parts.
The formal part is 'that by which the thing is actual'.
4
Apply to mental composition
The same logic applies to mental composites. Just as the formal part causes the external composite, it can serve as a defining element in the mind without circularity.
5
Note the inconsistency in the opponent's position
The commentator points out that the opponent's own view would imply that composites are not truly composite, contradicting the known fact that things like beds are composed of parts.
This is a critique of the opponent's argument, not a direct part of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لا بد أن يعرف شيئاً من أجزائها، وذلك إما نفسه أو غيره فيلزم أحد المحذورين كما مر لأنا نقول : معرف الماهية يجب أن يحصل معرفتها بوجه ما يميزها عما عداها، وليس يلزم من تحصيل معرفة شيء من أجزائها ألا يرى أن الجزء الصوري علة لحصول الماهية في الخارج، وليس علة الحصول شيء من أجزائها فيه ومن التزم ما ذكرتموه اختار تعريفه ذلك فلانه حينئذ يكون ترك التقيد بالخارجي مشعراً بأن ما ذكره يستلزم انتفاء التركيب عن المركبات مطلقاً، وأما ثالثاً فما فائدة الإشعار والحال أنه قد صرح فيما بعد بأن ما ذكره يستلزم انتفاء التركيب عن المركبات المعلومة التركيب قوله : ( لا يقال لا بد الخ استدلال آخر على امتناع التعريف ببعض الأجزاء . قوله : ( أن الجزء الصوري (الخ يعني أن الجزء الصوري في المركبات كالسرير، والبيت علة لحصول الماهية إذ الصوري ما به الشيء بالفعل وليس علة لحصول شيء من أجزاء المركب، أما للجزء المادي فلتقدمه على الصوري، وأما للصوري فلامتناع علية الشيء لنفسه، وإذا كان الجزء الصوري علة لحصول المركب الخارجي مع عدم كونه علة لشيء من أجزائه، فليجز مثل ذلك في المركب الذهني فإن الوجود الذهني بمثابة الوجود الخارجي
It is not said: It is necessary that it know something of its parts, and that is either itself or another, so one of the two difficulties follows as before. For we say: The definition of the quiddity must obtain knowledge of it in some way that distinguishes it from what is other than it, and it is not necessary from obtaining knowledge of something of its parts that you do not see that the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) is a cause (ʿilla) for the occurrence of the quiddity in the external, and it is not a cause for the occurrence of any of its parts therein. And whoever committed to what you mentioned chose his definition of it, so then his abandonment of restriction to the external implies that what he mentioned entails the negation of composition from compounds absolutely. And thirdly, what benefit is the implication when the state is that he declared later that what he mentioned entails the negation of composition from compounds known to be composite. His statement: (It is not said: It is necessary etc.) is another proof for the impossibility of definition by some parts. His statement: (That the formal part) means that the formal part in compounds like the bed and the house is a cause for the occurrence of the quiddity, since the formal is that by which the thing is actual, and it is not a cause for the occurrence of any part of the compound. As for the material part, due to its priority over the formal, and as for the formal, due to the impossibility of the causality of a thing for itself. And if the formal part is a cause for the occurrence of the external compound without being a cause for any of its parts, then let the like be permitted in the mental compound, for mental existence is like external existence.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against definition by some parts. The objector argues that if a quiddity is defined by some of its parts, those parts must themselves be known, leading to a vicious circle or infinite regress. The commentator refutes this by stating that the definition need only provide a distinguishing mark, not exhaustive knowledge of all parts. He then introduces the example of the formal part (al-juzʾ al-ṣūrī) in composite things like a bed or house. The formal part is the cause of the existence of the whole composite in the external world, but it is not the cause of any individual part (neither the material part nor itself). This shows that a part can define the whole without defining its parts. The same applies to mental composition. The commentator also criticizes the opponent's position by noting that it would imply that composites are not truly composite. Finally, he points out an inconsistency in the opponent's argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining a whole by a part requires prior knowledge of all parts, leading to circularity.
Correction: The formal part example shows that a part can define the whole without requiring knowledge of its own parts, as it is the cause of the composite but not of its parts.

Key Takeaway

What Is Kalam?

Objection and Response on Definition by an External Part
The commentator handles the objection that if the defining part is itself defined by something external, the problem recurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends definition by an external part against the objection of circularity, and the conditions required for such a definition to work.

Core Idea
Definition by an external property works only if it's necessary and specific, without needing detailed prior knowledge.

In this passage, the commentator tackles a tricky objection: if a defining part is itself defined by something external, doesn't that just push the problem further? He responds by showing that the same answer applies, and then offers a positive account of definition by an external necessary property. The key is that the property must be unique to the quiddity and that the mind can transition directly without needing detailed knowledge of the specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector argues that if the defining part is defined by something external, the problem of circularity or infinite regress returns. Notice how the objector frames this as a recurrence of the earlier difficulty.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قلت'.
2
Follow the initial answer
The commentator gives a brief initial response: the same answer (presumably the formal part example) applies again, so no new problem arises. Understand that this is a reaffirmation of a previous solution.
3
Examine the alternative approach
The commentator then presents a positive alternative: definition by an external necessary concomitant (lāzim). Pay attention to the conditions: the external must be necessary (lāzim) and specific (mukhtaṣṣ) to the quiddity, and the mind must transition from its concept to the quiddity's concept.
The phrase 'إن الخارج إذا كان لازماً لها مختصاً بها' captures the core conditions.
4
Understand the role of specification and knowledge
The commentator argues that knowledge of the specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) is not required for the transition; the relationship itself is enough. Even if knowledge were required, it only depends on a vague (ijmālī) conception of the quiddity, avoiding circularity. Study the example of a body's specificity to a particular space.
This step addresses the circularity objection directly.

لغيره الذي هو خارج عنه فإن قلت : إذا كان ذلك البعض المعرف للماهية معرفا بغيره كما ذكرتم عاد الإشكال بحذافيره إلى تعريفه به قلت : ويعود إليه أيضاً الجواب برمته ( أو ) نختار ( أنه أي تعريف الماهية ) بالخارج) عنها ( ويجب ) في تعريفه إياها ( الاختصاص ) فإن الخارج إذا كان لازماً لها مختصاً بها، وكان مع ذلك بحيث ينتقل الذهن من تصوره إلى تصورها صلح أن يكون معرفاً لها بلا لزوم محذور ( لا العلم به ) فإنه ليس شرطاً في ذلك الانتقال المرتب على الاختصاص، والعلاقة وهو المنشأ لما ذكرتموه من المحال ( وإن سلم وجوب العلم بالاختصاص في تعريف الخارج ( فالعلم بالاختصاص يتوقف على تصور الماهية بوجه (ما لا على تصورها الحاصل بتعريف الخارج إياها ( فلا دورو) يتوقف ) على تصور ما عداها باعتبار شامل له ) أي مجملاً ( لا ) على تصور ما عداها ( مفصلاً وإنه ) أي تصور ما عداها باعتبار شامل ممکن کاختصاص ) أي كعلمنا باختصاص ( الجسم بحيز معين ( دون ما عداه
To another that is external to it. For if you say: If that part which defines the quiddity is a definer by another as you mentioned, the problem returns entirely to its definition by it. I say: The entire answer also returns to it. Or we choose: That it, i.e., definition of the quiddity, is by something external to it, and specification (al-ikhtiṣāṣ) is necessary in its defining it. For the external, if it is necessary for it and specific to it, and moreover is such that the mind transitions from its conception to its conception, it is suitable to be a definer for it without a difficulty arising. Not knowledge of it (i.e., the specification), for it is not a condition in that transition which depends on specificity and correlation, which is the source of what you mentioned of impossibility. And if the necessity of knowledge of specificity in definition by the external is granted, then knowledge of specificity depends on the conception of the quiddity in some way, not on the conception of it obtained by the external's defining it. So there is no circularity. And it depends on the conception of what is other than it in a comprehensive manner, i.e., in summary, not on the detailed conception of what is other than it. And that, i.e., the comprehensive conception of what is other than it, is possible, like our knowledge of the specificity of a body to a particular space to the exclusion of what is other than it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on definition by some parts. The objector raises a new problem: if the part that defines the quiddity is itself defined by something external to it, then the same circularity or infinite regress applies to that part. The commentator responds that the same answer (the formal part example) applies again, so no new objection stands. Then the commentator presents a positive alternative: definition by an external thing (al-khārij) that is a necessary and specific property of the quiddity. For such a definition to work, it must be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) that is exclusive to that quiddity, and the mind must be able to transition from its concept to the concept of the quiddity. Importantly, the definer does not require the mind to have prior knowledge of the specificity; the transition happens automatically due to the relationship. Even if one insists that knowledge of the specificity is required, the commentator argues that such knowledge depends only on a vague (ijmālī) conception of the quiddity, not a detailed one, thus avoiding circularity. An example is our knowledge that a body is specific to a certain place without knowing all other bodies in detail.

Key Terms
الخارج
الاختصاص
اللازم
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any external property that is true of a quiddity can serve as a definition.
Correction: The external property must be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) that is specific (mukhtaṣṣ) to that quiddity and must enable the mind to transition directly to the quiddity's concept.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what condition is necessary for an external thing to serve as a definition of a quiddity?
A) It must be known to the definer in detail.
B) It must be a necessary concomitant specific to that quiddity and enable the mind to transition to the quiddity.
C) It must be a part of the quiddity's essence.
D) It must be the formal cause of the quiddity.

Key Takeaway
Definition by an external part works if that part is a necessary and specific property that leads the mind directly to the quiddity, without requiring prior detailed knowledge.

How would you know if your understanding leads directly to the truth without circular reasoning?

Conditions for Definition by an External and Refutation of Circularity
The commentator defines the condition of necessity and rebuts circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of necessity (luzūm) for definition by an external and how the commentator refutes charges of circularity.

Core Idea
Definition by an external requires a property that is present in every individual of the quiddity.

This passage from the commentary tackles a key condition for valid definition by an external: the property must be necessary (lāzim) in the sense of being inclusive of all individuals. It also addresses an objection that such definition leads to circularity, clarifying that the mind does not need prior detailed knowledge of the property's specificity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full literal translation. Let the commentator's argument flow naturally.
2
Focus on the meaning of necessity
Identify how the commentator defines 'necessity' (luzūm) for definition by an external. Notice that it means the property is inclusive (shāmil) of all individuals.
The commentator distinguishes between necessary and non-necessary specific properties.
3
Understand the refutation of circularity
Examine the objection about circularity and the commentator's response. Note that the problem of circularity only applies to definition by an external, and that knowledge of specificity is not required for every intermediate step.
The commentator argues that vague knowledge suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إذا كان لازماً لها أي شاملاً لجميع أفرادها فمعنى اللزوم للماهية ثبوته لها في ضمن جميع الأفراد بأن لا يوجد فرد من أفرادها بدونه فمآله إلى الشمول، وإنما حملناه على ذلك، إذ يجوز التعريف بالخاصة الغير اللازمة إذا كانت شاملة. قوله : (بحيث ينتقل الخ) وكان غافلاً عن اختصاصه وعدم اختصاصه، فلا يرد أنه لو لم يعلم الاختصاص احتمل عدم الاختصاص عنده فلا يفيد التعريف بالتمييز التام. قوله: (فإن قيل الخ استدلال آخر على امتناع الاكتساب في التصور بإبطال التعريف بالجزء والخارج بناء على أنه قد ثبت امتناع التعريف بنفسه. قوله : (عاد الإشكال بحذافيره حذافير الشيء أعاليه ونواحيه، ويقال: أعطاه الدنيا بحذافيرها أي بأسرها والواحد حذفاً، وأراد بالإشكال الإشكال المتعلق بالتعريف بالخارج، وهو لزوم الدور وإحاطة الذهن بما لا يتناهى لا الإشكال المتعلق بمطلق التعريف، حتى يرد أن أنه معرف بغيره فلا يحسن أن الغير إما نفسه أو غيره أو جزء أو خارج عنه الغرض. قوله : ( فالعلم بالاختصاص يتوقف على تصور الماهية بوجه ما الخ) فإن قلت : معرفة الاختصاص يقتضي كون ذلك الوجه مختصاً، فينقل الكلام إلى معرفة اختصاصه، فيدور أو يتسلسل قلت : المجيب إنما سلم وجوب معرفة الاختصاص في المعرف لا في كل وجه فتأمل
His statement: (If it is necessary for it) i.e., inclusive of all its individuals, so the meaning of necessity (luzūm) for the quiddity is its being established for it within all individuals, such that no individual of its individuals exists without it, so its outcome is inclusivity. We only interpreted it that way, because definition by a non-necessary specific property is permissible if it is inclusive. His statement: (Such that it transitions...) and he was heedless of its specificity and non-specificity, so it is not objected that if he did not know the specificity, the lack of specificity would be possible to him, and so the definition by complete distinction would be useless. His statement: (For if it is said...) is another proof on the impossibility of acquisition in conception by invalidating definition by the part and the external, based on the fact that the impossibility of definition by itself has been established. His statement: (The problem returns entirely) 'ḥadhāfīr' of a thing are its heights and sides, and it is said: 'He gave him the world with its ḥadhāfīr' i.e., entirely, singular 'ḥadhfan'. He intended by 'the problem' the problem related to definition by the external, which is the occurrence of circularity and the mind encompassing what is infinite, not the problem related to absolute definition, so that it is objected that it is a definer by another, so it is not good that the other is either itself or another or a part or external to it. His statement: (So knowledge of specificity depends on the conception of the quiddity in some way...) For if you say: Knowledge of specificity requires that way to be specific, so the discussion transfers to knowledge of its specificity, thus circularity or regress occurs. I say: The respondent only conceded the necessity of knowledge of specificity in the definer, not in every way. So contemplate.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that elaborates on the conditions for definition by an external (al-khārij). The commentator explains that 'necessity' (luzūm) here means the external property is inclusive of all individuals of the quiddity, so that no individual lacks it. He distinguishes between necessary and non-necessary specific properties, noting that even non-necessary ones can define if they are universally true. He then addresses an objection: if one does not know whether the property is specific, the definition fails. The commentator dismisses this by saying the objector overlooks that the definition doesn't require prior knowledge of specificity—the mind transitions directly. The commentary then discusses another argument about the impossibility of acquired conception, clarifying that the problem (circularity) applies only to definition by an external, not to definition by itself. Finally, he responds to a subtle objection: if knowledge of specificity requires a specific conception, then circularity or regress arises. The answer is that the necessity of knowledge only applies to the definer (the external) not to every intermediate conception; a vague knowledge suffices.

Definition by an external requires a property that is universally necessary, and the mind does not need prior detailed knowledge; circularity is avoided by restricting the requirement to the definer.

Defining Quiddities by External Attributes
Sanusi addresses the objection that definition by external leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how definition by external attributes avoids circularity by requiring exclusivity, necessity, and a mental transition, and that only a summary conception of exclusivity is needed.

Core Idea
Definition by external works when the attribute is exclusive and mentally triggers the quiddity.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a key objection to defining a quiddity by something external: if the external itself is defined by something else, does circularity return? He shows that the answer is no, provided the external is necessary and exclusive, and that the mind can transition from its concept to the quiddity's concept. Moreover, any required knowledge of that exclusivity only needs a summary conception, avoiding a vicious circle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the flow of the objection and response.
Notice the compact style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says: if the part that defines the quiddity is itself defined by something else, the original problem (circularity) returns. Mark where this is stated.
The objection begins with 'فإن قلت'.
3
Understand the conditions for definition by external
Sanusi lists three conditions: the external must be necessary (lazim), exclusive (mukhtass), and cause a mental transfer from its conception to the quiddity's conception.
These conditions ensure the definition is informative and non-circular.
4
See how circularity is avoided
Even if knowledge of exclusivity is required, it depends only on a summary (ijmali) conception of the quiddity, not the full detail obtained from the definition. This prevents a circle.
The summary conception is enough to know exclusivity, like knowing a body occupies space without analyzing all its properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For if the external is necessary for it and exclusive to it, and moreover is such that the mind transfers from its conception to the conception of it, it is suitable to be a definer of it without necessitating a problem. (Not knowledge of it) for it is not a condition in that transfer based on exclusivity and relation, which is the source of what you mentioned of impossibility. (And if knowledge of exclusivity in the definition by external is granted, then knowledge of exclusivity depends on conceiving the quiddity in some respect, not on the conception of it obtained by the definition by external. So there is no circle. It depends on conceiving what is other than it in a comprehensive respect, i.e., in summary, not on conceiving what is other than it in detail. And that is conceivable, like our knowledge of the exclusivity of a body to a specific place without what is other than it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding definition by something external. The objector says: if the part that defines the quiddity is itself defined by something else, the original problem (circularity) returns. The responder replies that the same answer applies, or chooses the position that definition by external is valid provided the external is necessary (lazim) for the quiddity, exclusive (mukhtass) to it, and such that the mind transitions from its concept to the quiddity's concept. Knowledge of the exclusivity is not required for the transition; the transition is based on the objective relation. Even if knowledge of exclusivity were required, that knowledge depends only on a partial conception of the quiddity, not the full conception obtained by the definition, thus avoiding circularity. The conception of the external can be summary (ijmali) rather than detailed, and possible.

Objection to Definition by Inner and Outer Parts
Sanusi presents an objection against definition by inner/outer parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against definition by parts, which argues that such definition leads to impossibility or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Definition by parts either assumes what is already known or leads to infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a powerful objection against defining something by its inner or outer parts. The objection systematically examines three possibilities for the status of those parts: necessary knowledge, no knowledge, or acquired knowledge. Each case is shown to be problematic, challenging the very possibility of definition through parts. Let's explore the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: three cases are examined.
2
Identify the three cases
The objection considers three possibilities for the inner/outer parts: they are necessarily known, not obtained, or acquired. Find each in the text.
The cases are marked by 'إن كانت حاصلة ضرورة', 'إذا لم تكن حاصلة', and 'إذا لم يكن حصولها ضرورياً بل كسبياً'.
3
Understand the first case
If the parts are necessarily known and entail knowledge of the quiddity, then the quiddity is already known. Defining it by them would be 'obtaining what is already obtained', which is impossible.
This is the principle of tahsil al-hasil.
4
Understand the second case
If the parts are not obtained at all, then definition by them is absolutely inconceivable because there is no knowledge to base definition on.
This case is straightforward.
5
Understand the third case
If the parts are acquired (kasbi), they themselves need a definition, leading to another definer. This either continues infinitely (tasalsul, impossible) or ends in something necessarily known.
This shows the dependence on a foundation of necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأمور الداخلة أو الخارجة إن كانت حاصلة ضرورة ومستلزمة للعلم بالماهية فالماهية معلومة معها فلا تعرف الماهية لها لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، وإلا امتنع التعريف بها أما إذا لم تكن حاصلةً، فلا يتصور التعريف بها قطعاً، وأما إذا لم يكن حصولها ضرورياً، بل كسبياً فلاحتياجها حينئذ إلى معرف آخر، وينقل الكلام إليه فإما أن يتسلسل وهو محال، أو ينتهي إلى ما حصوله ضروري
If it is said: The inner matters or the outer matters, if they are obtained necessarily and entail knowledge of the quiddity, then the quiddity is known along with them, so the quiddity is not defined by them because of the impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained. Otherwise, definition by them is impossible. For if they are not obtained, then definition by them is absolutely inconceivable. And if their obtainment is not necessary but rather acquired, then they require another definer, and the discussion transfers to it. Either there is an infinite regress, which is impossible, or it ends in what is obtained necessarily.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against definition by inner or outer parts. The objector argues: if the inner or outer matters are already necessarily known and entail knowledge of the quiddity, then the quiddity is already known, so defining it by them is impossible (since you cannot obtain what is already obtained). If they are not obtained, definition is impossible because they are unknown. If they are acquired (kasbi), they need another definer, leading to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or ending in necessary knowledge. The objection aims to show that no conception can be acquired through definition by parts, thus attacking the possibility of definition itself. The rest of the passage contains commentary on the conditions, but the core objection is as given.

Key Terms
أمور داخلة
أمور خارجة
تحصيل الحاصل
تسلسل
ضروري
كسبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the defining parts are necessary, definition is merely redundant but still possible.
Correction: The objection shows it is not just redundant but impossible, because obtaining what is already obtained is logically impossible (tahsil al-hasil).

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what happens if the defining inner/outer parts are obtained through acquisition (kasb)?
A) Definition becomes certain.
B) They require another definer, leading to infinite regress or ending in necessary knowledge.
C) They are sufficient by themselves.
D) They cause circularity.

Key Takeaway
Definition by parts fails because it either assumes what is already known or leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection shows that definition cannot provide new knowledge; it only reorganizes what is already known. How does this challenge your reliance on definitions in your study of Islam? Are you content with definitions that simply restate what you already accept, or do you seek deeper certainty?
?
Infinite regress is impossible; there must be a foundation of necessary knowledge. What is that foundation for you personally? Have you examined whether your core beliefs rest on necessary knowledge or on mere assumptions?

How Definition Is Possible: The Answer to the Objection
Jurjani answers the objection that definition requires prior knowledge of the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that definition requires prior knowledge of the essence is answered through the concept of kasb (acquisition) that gathers and orders the parts of the definition.

Core Idea
Definition works by gathering and ordering scattered parts, not by requiring full prior knowledge.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a fundamental challenge to the very possibility of definition: if defining something requires already knowing what it is, then definition seems circular or impossible. He provides a nuanced answer that distinguishes between having scattered knowledge of parts and deliberately assembling them into a complete conception. This answer is crucial for understanding how definitions function in kalam and logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that definition requires prior knowledge of the essence, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Recognize why this seems problematic.
The objection is a shubha (sophistical argument) that must be refuted.
2
Grasp the core answer
The answer is that knowledge of the essence comes from having its parts present together and ordered. This presence and order is achieved through kasb (acquisition).
Kasb is an act of gathering and arranging, not a passive reception.
3
See the elaboration
The parts (internal or external) are already known but scattered and mixed. Kasb gathers all parts and arranges them, forming a composite conception that is the true knowledge of the essence.
Even partial gathering yields a more complete conception than before.
4
Consider the case of simple meanings
A possible objection: this answer doesn't apply to simple meanings without parts. The reply: such simple meanings may be present but not deliberately noticed; when brought to focal awareness, they yield knowledge, though this is rare.
This shows the answer covers even the difficult case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للعلم بالماهية فامتناع التعريف بها ظاهر (قلنا) في الجواب عن هذه الشبهة (المستلزم) للعلم بالماهية حضورها معاً مرتبة وأنه (أي ذلك الحضور مع) الاجتماع والترتيب (بالكسب) وتفصيله أن الأمور الداخلة أو الخارجة حاصلة، إما ضرورة وإما اكتساباً منتهياً إلى الضرورة، لكنها متفرقة مخلوطة بأمور أخر، فإذا جمع الأجزاء بأسرها ورتبت حصل مجموع هو تصور الماهية كنهها، وهذا المجموع إنما حصل بالكسب الذي هو جمع تلك الأجزاء وترتيبها، وكذا إذا جمع بعض متعدد من أجزائها ورتب بعضه مع بعض فإنه يحصل مجموع هو تصور الماهية بوجه أكمل مما كان قبل ذلك، وقس على هذه الأمور الخارجية المتعددة فإن قلت: هذا الجواب لا يتأتى في التعريف بالمعاني البسيطة قلت: من جوز ذلك فله أن يقول: إن المعاني البسيطة الحاصلة قد لا تكون ملحوظة قصداً فإذا استحضرت ولوحظت قصداً أفادت العلم بالماهية وإن كان ذلك نادراً جداً.
For knowledge of the essence, the impossibility of defining it is apparent. (We say) in answer to this objection: that which necessitates knowledge of the essence is its presence together and ordered, and that (i.e., that presence) involves conjunction and arrangement through acquisition (kasb). Its detail is that the internal or external matters are obtained, either necessarily or through acquisition ending in the necessary, but they are scattered and mixed with other things. So when all the parts are gathered and arranged, a composite results which is the conception of the essence in its reality. This composite is only obtained through acquisition, which is the gathering and arrangement of those parts. Similarly, if some multiple of its parts are gathered and some are arranged with others, a composite results which is a more complete conception of the essence than before. And apply this to multiple external matters. If you say: this answer does not apply to definition by simple meanings, I say: whoever allows that may say that the simple meanings that are obtained may not be deliberately noticed; but when they are called to mind and deliberately noticed, they yield knowledge of the essence, even if that is very rare.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a shubha (objection) about the possibility of definition. The objection is that defining something requires prior knowledge of its essence, leading to circularity or infinite regress. The answer distinguishes between the scattered presence of parts (internal and external) and their gathered and arranged presence. The scattered parts, obtained either necessarily or through acquisition ending in the necessary, do not give knowledge of the essence. But when they are collected and ordered through a deliberate act of kasb (acquisition), a composite conception of the essence is formed. This composite is the true mental representation of the essence. The author then extends this to multiple external items. He then raises an objection: this answer does not work for simple meanings (ma'ani basita) that have no parts. The reply is that simple meanings may be present but not deliberately noted; when they are brought to focal awareness (istihdar and mulahaza qasdan), they yield knowledge of the essence, though this is rare. Thus, the answer preserves the possibility of definition by emphasizing the role of acquisition in bringing together and ordering the constituents of the definition.

Key Terms
shubha
ta'rif
mahiyya
hudur
kasb
ma'ani basita

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition is impossible because it requires complete prior knowledge of the essence.
Correction: Definition does not require complete prior knowledge; it works by assembling already-known parts (scattered) through deliberate acquisition (kasb) to form a complete conception.

Checkpoint
What is the role of 'kasb' in obtaining knowledge of the essence according to this passage?
A) It is the direct cause of knowledge without any gathering of parts.
B) It gathers and orders the scattered parts to form a composite conception.
C) It replaces the necessity of parts altogether.
D) It is unnecessary because knowledge is always immediate.

Key Takeaway
Definition is possible because its parts can be known separately and then deliberately gathered and ordered through kasb to form a complete conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new concept, do you often feel you need complete understanding before you can define it? How does this passage challenge you to instead gather and order the pieces of knowledge you already have?
?
Think of a matter of faith you struggled to define clearly. In light of this lesson, what 'scattered parts' of knowledge did you already possess? Will you now deliberately gather and arrange them through study and reflection?
?
This passage says simple meanings can be known by deliberate notice. Is there a simple truth about Allah or the unseen that you have not yet turned your full attention to? What would it mean for you to deliberately notice it today?

Proving Necessary Sciences and Their Threefold Division
Author enumerates the three types of necessary sciences and notes the limitation of one type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary sciences are foundational to all acquired knowledge and how they are divided into three categories: introspective, sensory, and intuitive.

Core Idea
All acquired knowledge rests on necessary sciences that must be proven and divided into three types.

This passage opens the Fourth Observatory of al-Mawaqif, where the author lays the foundation for all subsequent theological reasoning. He argues that necessary sciences are the ultimate reference point for all acquired knowledge, and then provides a crucial classification into three types. He also explains why introspective knowledge, though certain, cannot serve as a universal proof because it is not known to be shared.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the author's argument. Notice the structure: first a statement of need, then a division into three categories, then a note on one category's limitation.
The passage includes both the main text and commentary. Focus on the core claims.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage divides necessary sciences into three types: introspective (wijdāniyyāt), sensory (ḥissiyyāt), and intuitive (badīhiyyāt). Find where each is introduced and how they are defined.
The sensory category is further subdivided into experiential, recurrent, imaginative, intuitive, and observed.
3
Understand the limitation of introspective sciences
The author states that introspective sciences are of little benefit for proof because they are not known to be shared. Reflect on why this is a problem: if you cannot be sure someone else has the same inner experience, you cannot use it as evidence.
This is why sensory and intuitive sciences are considered the mainstay for public proof.
4
Summarize the key takeaway
Write a one-sentence summary of what this passage teaches about necessary sciences. Then compare with the key takeaway provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع في إثبات العلوم الضرورية من المبادئ أي بيان ثبوتها وتحققها والرد على منكريها ولا بد لنا من ذلك (إذ إليه المنتهى ) فإن العلوم الكسبية العقائد الدينية وغيرها تنتهي إليها وهي الأولى ولولاها لم نتحصل على علم أصلاً ) وأنها تنقسم إلى الوجدانيات ) وهي التي نجدها إما بنفوسنا، أو بالاتنا الباطنة كعلمنا بوجود ذواتنا وخوفنا، وغضبنا ولذتنا، وألمنا وجوعنا وشبعنا . ( وإنها قليلة النفع في العلوم لأنها غير مشتركة ) أي غير معلومة الاشتراك يقيناً ( فلا تقوم حجة على الغير فإن ذلك الغير ربما لم يجد من باطنه ما وجدناه ( وإلى الحسيات ( أراد بها ما للحس مدخل فيها فيتناول التجربيات والمتواترات، وأحكام الوهم في المحسوسات والحدسيات والمشاهدات ( والبديهيات ) قوله : أي بيان ثبوتها وتحققها ليس المراد تحققها في نفسها فقط بل حصولها في النفس واتصافها بها . قوله : ( وأنها تنقسم (الخ بفتح الهمزة عطف على إثبات العلوم فهو كالتفسير له أي إثبات انقسامها إلى أقسامها المذكورة، وقوله : إنها قليلة بكسر الهمزة جملة معترضة لبيان عدم التعرض لإثباتها وكذلك قوله فهذان القسمان هما العمدة معترضة بالفاء سبب التعرض لإثباتها والرد على منكريها .
The fourth observatory: on proving the necessary sciences. This is a brief statement of their existence and realization and the refutation of those who deny them. We must do this because they are the ultimate point of reference (إذ إليه المنتهى), since the acquired sciences, religious beliefs and others, end with them, and they are the first principles; without them we would not obtain any knowledge at all. And that they are divided into: (1) introspective (al-wijdāniyyāt): those we find either by ourselves or by our inner faculties, such as our knowledge of our own existence, fear, anger, pleasure, pain, hunger, and satiety. (2) And that they are of little benefit in the sciences because they are not shared (غير مشتركة), i.e., not known to be shared with certainty, so they do not constitute a proof against others, for that other may not find in his inner self what we found. (3) And into the sensory (al-ḥissiyyāt): meaning those in which the senses have a role, including the experiential (tajribiyyāt), the recurrent (mutawātirāt), the judgments of imagination in sensibles, the intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt), and the observed. (4) And the intuitive (al-badīhiyyāt). [commentary] His statement: 'i.e., a brief statement of their existence and realization' – the intent is not only their existence in themselves, but their occurrence in the soul and the soul's being described by them. His statement: 'and that they are divided' (with fatha on the hamza) is coordinated with 'proving the sciences' and is an interpretation of it, i.e., proving their division into the mentioned divisions. And his statement: 'and that they are of little benefit' (with kasra on the hamza) is a parenthetical sentence to explain that he does not intend to prove them, and likewise his statement 'these two divisions are the mainstay' is a parenthetical with 'fa' to explain the reason for undertaking to prove them and refute deniers.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth observatory with the aim of proving the necessary sciences. The author first states that these sciences are foundational (المبادئ) and that all acquired knowledge depends on them. He then divides them into three categories: introspective (wijdāniyyāt), sensory (ḥissiyyāt), and intuitive (badīhiyyāt). The commentary clarifies that the proof is not merely of their existence but of their actual presence in the soul. The author also notes that introspective sciences are of limited benefit because they are not known to be shared with others, so they cannot serve as proof. The sensory sciences include multiple subtypes such as experiential, recurrent, imaginative, intuitive, and observational. The commentary explains that the statement about the division is connected to the initial claim, and the remark about their limited benefit is a parenthetical note to indicate that he is not proving those.

Correction: The author shows that even necessary sciences need to be proven and categorized because not all of them are universally accessible (e.g., introspective).

D) Because they are theoretical.

Key Takeaway
The necessary sciences are the foundation of all knowledge, and they are of three types: introspective, sensory, and intuitive, with only the last two serving as universal proofs.

Classification of Necessary Sciences and the Status of Inner Experiences
Al-Jurjani defines necessary sciences, divides them into three categories, and explains why inner experiences are not reliable shared proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of necessary sciences and why inner experiences (wijdāniyyāt) cannot serve as universal proofs in theological argumentation.

Core Idea
Inner experiences are personal and not shareable as certain proofs.

Before we can build arguments in theology, we must establish the foundation of all knowledge: the necessary sciences. Al-Jurjani introduces these as the first principles from which all acquired sciences derive. He then divides them into three kinds, focusing on why one of them—inner experiences—cannot be used as a universal proof. This distinction is crucial for understanding how rational argumentation works in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the English translation. Note the flow: the author states the need to establish necessary sciences, then divides them, then explains the limitation of one division.
Pay attention to the technical terms: وجدانيات, حسيات, بديهيات.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage lists three divisions of necessary sciences: inner experiences (وجدانيات), sensed things (حسيات), and self-evidents (بديهيات). Write them down and note how each is defined.
The حسيات include subcategories like empiricals and recurrent reports.
3
Focus on the argument about inner experiences
Why are وجدانيات of little benefit? The author says they are 'not shared' (غير مشتركة). The commentator clarifies this means 'not known with certainty to be shared'. This limits their use as proofs for others.
Example: My hunger is certain for me, but I cannot prove it to you with certainty.
4
Understand the nuance in the commentary
The commentator notes that some inner experiences (like our knowledge of our own existence) are actually known to be shared, but even then they are rarely used as proofs. The phrase 'ربما' (perhaps) softens the claim.
This subtlety shows the precision of kalām reasoning.
5
Reflect on why this matters
This passage establishes that for shared theological proofs, we must rely on the other two categories (حسيات and بديهيات) rather than personal experiences. It sets the stage for the rest of the discussion on necessary sciences.
This is foundational for all subsequent arguments in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع في إثبات العلوم الضرورية من المبادئ أي بيان ثبوتها وتحققها والرد على منكريها ولا بد لنا من ذلك (إذ إليه المنتهى ) فإن العلوم الكسبية العقائد الدينية وغيرها تنتهي إليها وهي الأولى ولولاها لم نتحصل على علم أصلاً ) وأنها تنقسم إلى الوجدانيات ) وهي التي نجدها إما بنفوسنا، أو بالاتنا الباطنة كعلمنا بوجود ذواتنا وخوفنا، وغضبنا ولذتنا، وألمنا وجوعنا وشبعنا . ( وإنها قليلة النفع في العلوم لأنها غير مشتركة ) أي غير معلومة الاشتراك يقيناً ( فلا تقوم حجة على الغير فإن ذلك الغير ربما لم يجد من باطنه ما وجدناه ( وإلى الحسيات ( أراد بها ما للحس مدخل فيها فيتناول التجربيات والمتواترات، وأحكام الوهم في المحسوسات والحدسيات والمشاهدات ( والبديهيات )
قوله : أي بيان ثبوتها وتحققها ليس المراد تحققها في نفسها فقط بل حصولها في النفس واتصافها بها .
قوله : ( وأنها تنقسم (الخ بفتح الهمزة عطف على إثبات العلوم فهو كالتفسير له أي إثبات انقسامها إلى أقسامها المذكورة، وقوله : إنها قليلة بكسر الهمزة جملة معترضة لبيان عدم التعرض لإثباتها وكذلك قوله فهذان القسمان هما العمدة معترضة بالفاء سبب التعرض لإثباتها والرد على منكريها .
قوله : ( وإنها قليلة (النفع لإفادتها العلم لصاحب الوجدان .
قوله : أي غير معلومة (الاشتراك صرف المتن عن ظاهره لأن غاية الأمر عدم العلم بالاشتراك لا انتفاء الاشتراك ثم عدم العلم أيضاً أكثري، وإلا فبعض الوجدانيات معلوم الاشتراك كعلمنا بوجود ذواتنا، ولذا يستدل بالوجدان في بعض المطالب ولقلته وندرته لم يصح بكونها حجة عند اشتراك الوجدان، وأشار إليه بكلمة ربما، وقال المصنف : إنها قليلة النفع لقلة مواد اشتراكها .
The Fourth Observatory on the Establishment of the Necessary Sciences from the principles, i.e., an exposition of their existence and realization and a refutation of those who deny them, and we need that (for to it is the end). For the acquired sciences, religious doctrines and others, end at them, and they are the first; without them we would not obtain any knowledge at all. And that they are divided into inner experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt), which are what we find either by our souls or by our inner faculties, like our knowledge of the existence of our selves, our fear, anger, pleasure, pain, hunger, and thirst. And that they are of little benefit in the sciences because they are not shared (ghayr mushtaraka), i.e., not known with certainty to be shared, so they do not constitute a proof against another, for that other perhaps does not find in his inner self what we find. And into the sensed things (al-ḥissiyyāt), by which he meant what sensation has a part in, thus including empiricals, recurrent reports, judgments of the estimative faculty regarding sensed things, intuitive inferences (al-ḥadsiyyāt), and observations. And into the self-evidents (al-badīhiyyāt).
His saying: 'i.e., an exposition of their existence and realization': The intent is not their realization in themselves only, but their occurrence in the soul and the soul's being described by them.
His saying: 'And that they are divided' etc., with a fatha on the hamza, is coordinate to 'the establishment of the sciences' and is an interpretation of it, i.e., the establishment of their division into the mentioned divisions. And his saying: 'they are little' with a kasra on the hamza is an intervening sentence to indicate that there is no need to establish them, and similarly his saying: 'these two divisions are the mainstays' is an intervening sentence with a fa' that gives the reason for undertaking to establish them and refute their deniers.
His saying: 'And that they are of little benefit' because it yields knowledge only for the one who experiences it.
His saying: 'i.e., not known to be shared' diverts the text from its apparent meaning, because the utmost is lack of knowledge of sharing, not the negation of sharing. Then lack of knowledge is also for the most part, otherwise some inner experiences are known to be shared, like our knowledge of the existence of our selves; therefore one argues using inner experience in some matters, but due to its scarcity and rarity, it is not valid as a proof when the sharing of the experience exists. He indicated this by the word 'perhaps', and the author said: they are of little benefit due to the scarcity of their materials of commonality.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of necessary sciences. It begins by stating the necessity of establishing these sciences. The author then divides them into three categories: inner experiences (وجدانيات), sensed things (حسيات), and self-evidents (بديهيات). For each, he explains their nature and evidential value. The core argument is that وجدانيات, such as one's own hunger or fear, are not shared across individuals with certainty, so they cannot serve as a proof for others. The commentator clarifies that the text's 'not shared' is interpreted as 'not known to be shared', because absolute negation of sharing cannot be asserted. He notes that some وجدانيات (like self-existence) are known to be shared, but even then they are rarely used as proofs. Thus the mainstays for proof are the other two divisions.

الحسيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All inner experiences are completely useless in argumentation.
Correction: The text says they are 'not known to be shared with certainty,' but some inner experiences (like knowledge of existence) are in fact shared, though rarely used as proofs.

Checkpoint

B) They are not real knowledge

Key Takeaway
The necessary sciences are foundational, but inner experiences are not reliable as universal proofs because their sharing cannot be established with certainty.
