Sensory Judgment Cannot Give Universal Knowledge
Establishes why sensory judgment cannot yield universal judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory perception cannot provide universal judgments and the distinction between real and external universals.

Core Idea
Sensory perception only grasps present individuals; it cannot reach past, future, or imagined ones.

In this passage, the author explains a fundamental limitation of sensory knowledge. Even if we could perceive every instance of fire at a single moment, that perception would not cover all fires across time or hypothetical fires. This demonstrates why sense experience alone cannot ground universal truths, a key point in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption. Then read the translation. Let the author's reasoning unfold.
Notice the structure: observation → limitation → extension to imagined → conclusion.
2
Identify the three key points
The author argues: 1) Sensory perception is limited to present individuals. 2) Even if it could perceive all present, it cannot cover past, future, or imagined individuals. 3) Therefore, sensory judgment cannot be universal.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If sensory knowledge cannot yield universal statements, how can we claim universal truths about fire or anything else? This leads into the role of rational judgment in kalam.
This lesson sets the stage for understanding the necessity of rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع ثبات العلوم الضرورية النار، وتلك النار لا جميع النيران الموجودة في الحال ولو فرض إدراكه إياها بأسرها فليس له تعلق قطعاً بأفرادها الماضية والمستقبلة، فلا يعطي حكماً كلياً على جميع أفرادها ( سيما وقد ذهب المحققون إلى أن الحكم في قولنا النار حارة ليس على كل نار موجودة في الخارج في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة فقط بل عليها وعلى جميع (الأفراد المتوهمة الوجود في الخارج أيضاً ولا شك أنه لا تعلق للحس بها ) أي بالأفراد المتوهمة ( البتة ) فكيف يعطي حكماً متناولاً إياها؟ والحاصل أن الحكم لا يعطي حكماً كلياً أصلاً لا حقيقياً ولا خارجياً، فلا يتصور اعتبار حكمه في الكليات قطعاً
The fourth observation: establishing necessary sciences. Fire, and that fire is not all existing fires at present, and even if we suppose perception of all of them entirely, it has no connection at all to its past and future individuals, so it does not give a universal judgment over all its individuals (especially since the investigators have held that the judgment in our statement 'fire is hot' is not only about every fire existing externally in one of the three times, but also about all (imagined individuals existing externally) and there is no doubt that the sense has no connection to them) i.e., to the imagined individuals (at all), so how does it give a judgment encompassing them? The summary is that sense judgment does not give a universal judgment at all, neither real nor external, so considering its judgment in universals is absolutely inconceivable.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage argues that sensory perception cannot produce universal judgments. It uses the example of fire: even if a person perceived all existing fires at a given moment, that perception does not cover past and future fires. Moreover, investigators add that judgments about fire's heat apply to possible (imagined) fires as well, which the senses cannot reach. Therefore, sensory judgment yields no universal truth, whether 'real' (regarding actual existents across time) or 'external' (regarding all possible individuals). The conclusion is that sensory judgments cannot be considered reliable for universal statements.

Key Terms

حكم حسي
أفراد متوهمة
حقيقة
خارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory induction can ground universal laws.
Correction: Senses cannot cover all instances, especially hypothetical ones, so universal laws require rational judgment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does sensory judgment fail to give a universal judgment?
A) Because senses are limited to present individuals and cannot cover past, future, or imagined ones.
B) Because fire is hot only at present.
C) Because the investigators disagreed on the example.
D) Because universal judgments require reason alone.

Key Takeaway
Sensory judgment, being limited to present individuals and unable to cover past, future, or imagined ones, cannot yield universal truths; this underscores the need for rational judgment in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about the world that you consider certain. How much of that certainty is based on your senses? Does this lesson demonstrate that your senses cannot guarantee that belief covers all possible cases? What would it mean for your belief if it relied solely on sensory evidence?
?
Can you recall a situation where you assumed that because you have observed something repeatedly, it must always be true? How does this passage challenge that assumption? What would it take for your assumption to be rationally justified?
?
Consider your inner mental images of objects not present—like a fire you imagine in your mind. Why should your sensory experience give you any knowledge about that imagined fire? Do you ever trust your senses more than reason? Where in your life could that over-reliance be a problem?

The Principle of Non-Contradiction as the Clearest Self-Evident Truth
The author states the principle of non-contradiction and asserts its primacy among self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principle of non-contradiction is considered the clearest and strongest of self-evident truths in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The principle of non-contradiction is the most basic self-evident truth upon which all rational knowledge depends.

This passage introduces the fourth observation in establishing necessary sciences. It begins by stating the principle of non-contradiction – that a thing either exists or does not exist – and affirms that this principle is the clearest of self-evident truths. The author explains that those who recognize self-evident truths point to this principle as the prime example. This lesson unpacks why this principle holds such a foundational place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the principle
Find the statement of the principle of non-contradiction. It is the claim that a thing either exists or does not exist, and that these two cannot both be true or both false.
3
Understand its primacy
Notice that the author says this principle is the clearest self-evident truth. Ask yourself why it is considered the foremost example by those who study self-evident truths. The commentary will help answer this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية في الجزم قولنا ( الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ( أعني الترديد بين النفي والإثبات بأنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان ( وإنه غير يقيني أما الأول) وهو كونه أجلى البديهيات وأقواها ( فلان المعترفين بها أي بالبديهيات ) يمثلون لها بهذا الترديد تلك بين النفي والإثبات ) وثلاثة أخرى تتوقف تلك الثلاثة ( عليه الأول ) من الثلاثة المتوقفة عليه قولنا ( الكل أعظم من الجزء (وإلا أي وإن لم يكن أعظم ( فالجزء الآخر معتبر) في الكل لأنه جزء الكل ( وليس بمعتبر) فيه لحصول الاكتفاء بالجزء الأول، إذ المفروض أن الكل ليس أزيد منه، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات ( الثاني ) تلك الثلاثة قولنا (الأشياء المساوية) في الكمية مثلاً ( لشيء واحد متساوية ) في الكمية ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن متساوية في الكمية (فحقيقتها) في الكمية (واحدة) من قوله : ( أعني الترديد إلخ) أشار بالعناية إلى أن المراد بالكون واللاكون أعم من المحمولي، والرابطي كما يستفاد من بيان توقف القضايا الثلاثة وإلى أن الانفصال بينهما حقيقي، والمراد بالنفي والإثبات الانتفاء والثبوت لا الانتزاع والإيقاع، لأنهما يرتفعان عن الشك، وفي ذلك رد لما في شرح المقاصد حين جعل القول المذكور تفسيراً للترديد المذكور .
The fourth observation: on establishing necessary sciences. Concerning certainty, our saying: 'A thing either exists or does not exist' – meaning the disjunction between negation and affirmation, that they do not coexist and do not both cease – and it is not certain. As for the first, that it is the clearest and strongest of self-evident truths, it is because those who acknowledge self-evident truths give as an example this disjunction between negation and affirmation. And three other [propositions] depend on it. The first of the three that depend on it is our saying: 'The whole is greater than the part' – otherwise, if it were not greater, then the other part would be considered in the whole because it is a part of the whole, and not considered in it because sufficiency is obtained by the first part, since it is assumed that the whole is not more than it, so negation and affirmation would coexist. The second of the three is our saying: 'Things equal to one thing in quantity, for example, are equal in quantity' – otherwise, if they were not equal in quantity, then their essence in quantity would be one. From his saying: 'I mean the disjunction etc.', he indicated by careful attention that what is meant by 'existing' and 'not existing' is more general than the predicative and connective, as can be understood from the explanation of the dependence of the three propositions, and that the separation between them is real, and what is meant by 'negation and affirmation' is negation and affirmation themselves, not derivation and imposition, because they are above doubt. In that is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid when it made that saying an interpretation of the mentioned disjunction.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth observation (al-marsad al-rabi') on establishing necessary sciences. It begins by stating the self-evident principle of non-contradiction: a thing either exists or does not exist, and these two cannot both be true or both false. It then notes that this principle is not certain in the sense of being derived from senses but rather is the clearest and strongest of self-evident truths, as acknowledged by those who study them. The author then lists three other self-evident propositions that depend on this principle: first, that the whole is greater than the part; second, that things equal to the same thing are equal to each other; and third (implied later), that one body cannot be in two places at once. The commentary on the phrase 'I mean the disjunction' clarifies that 'existing' and 'not existing' here are not limited to predicative or connective senses but are general. The separation between them is real, and 'negation and affirmation' refer to actual absence and presence, not mental operations, because they are above doubt. This refutes a claim in Sharh al-Maqasid that interpreted the disjunction differently. The passage sets up the framework for the subsequent detailed discussion of each proposition.

Key Takeaway

Self-Evident Propositions Dependent on Non-Contradiction
The author enumerates three propositions that depend on the disjunction and explains their reliance on the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three self-evident propositions that depend on the principle of non-contradiction and how they illustrate its foundational role.

Core Idea
The whole is greater than the part, and things equal to the same thing are equal – these depend on non-contradiction.

After establishing the primacy of the principle of non-contradiction, the author lists three other self-evident propositions that logically depend on it. These are: the whole is greater than the part, things equal to the same thing are equal, and a third implied one (one body cannot be in two places at once). This lesson explains these propositions and their dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the propositions

2
Understand the dependence on non-contradiction

3

Clarifying the Meaning of 'Existing' and 'Not Existing'
The author clarifies the intended meaning of 'existing' and 'not existing' and refutes a misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the disjunction between existence and non-existence and how it refutes a misinterpretation in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Core Idea
The disjunction is about actual existence and non-existence, not mental operations.

The passage then offers a critical clarification about the terms 'existing' and 'not existing' in the principle of non-contradiction. The author explains that these terms are general, not limited to predicative or connective senses, and that the separation between them is real. Moreover, 'negation' and 'affirmation' refer to actual absence and presence, not to mental acts of denying or asserting. This serves as a refutation of an earlier interpretation in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification part
Read the Arabic text that provides the commentary on the disjunction. Note the technical terms used.
2
Understand the distinctions
Pay attention to the distinctions between predicative/connective senses, real separation, and the difference between actual states and mental operations.
3
Recognize the refutation
See how this precise understanding refutes the interpretation in Sharh al-Maqasid that considered the disjunction as a mere mental operation.

5 min
Non-Existence as a Conceptual Combination
Al-Jurjani answers the first three objections by analyzing the concept of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani resolves the apparent conflict in self-evident propositions by showing that the concept of non-existence is a restrictive combination, not an actual entity.

Core Idea
Non-existence is a mental concept, not an actual thing.

Al-Jurjani now addresses the objections that seem to undermine the certainty of self-evident propositions. He argues that the problem arises from misunderstanding the term 'non-existent' as referring to an actual entity. Instead, it is a conceptual combination that does not imply any external reality, making the dispute merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice how the author defines the concept of non-existence.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where Al-Jurjani says the concept is a restrictive combination (tarkīb taqyīdī). Understand that this means it is a mental construct, not a real entity.
3
Understand why it resolves the objection
Realize that if non-existence were an actual attribute, it would imply a contradiction. By treating it as a concept, the objection dissolves, and the dispute becomes verbal.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider how careful conceptual analysis in kalam prevents misunderstandings and preserves the certainty of self-evident propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب إن المتصور مفهوم المعدوم وذلك لأن المعدوم وقع هناك محمولاً فيراد به مفهومه وهو مفهوم قولنا ذات ما ثبت له العدم على أنه تركيب تقييدي لا أن ثمة ذاتاً ثبت له العدم في نفس الأمر وإلا اقتضى مفهوم المعدوم تحقق ذات في نفس الأمر متصفة بالعدم فيها وأنه باطل وهو أي مفهوم المعدوم هو المتميز لكونه [؟] والمعدوم بما لا وجود له أصلاً وأن النزاع بين الفريقين لفظي وهو المذكور في شرح المقاصد.
And the answer is that what is conceived is the concept of non-existence (al-maʿdūm), and that is because non-existence occurs there as a predicate, so what is meant is its concept, which is the concept of our statement 'a thing to which non-existence is attributed' as a restrictive combination (tarkīb taqyīdī); not that there is a thing to which non-existence is attributed in external reality, for otherwise the concept of non-existence would necessitate the realization of a thing in external reality qualified by non-existence in it, and that is false. And it—i.e., the concept of non-existence—is the distinguished one (al-mutamayyiz) because [?] and the non-existent is that which has no existence at all, and the dispute between the two groups is verbal, and that is mentioned in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage contains the answer (al-jawāb) to the first three objections raised against the certainty of self-evident propositions. The author explains that when we say 'non-existent' (al-maʿdūm) as a predicate, we are referring to its concept (mafhūm), not to any external reality. This concept is defined as 'a thing to which non-existence is attributed' (dhāt mā thabata lahu al-ʿadam), but this is a restrictive combination (tarkīb taqyīdī)—a conceptual relationship, not a real one. It does not imply that there is actually a thing in external reality that possesses non-existence, because that would be contradictory (bāṭil). To avoid this, the concept of non-existence is merely a mental distinction (tamayyuz). Thus, the dispute between those who affirm and deny a middle state is merely verbal (lafẓī), as mentioned in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid. The answer preserves the immediate certainty of self-evident propositions by showing that the apparent problem arises from misunderstanding the conceptual nature of terms.

Key Terms
المعدوم
المفهوم
التركيب التقيدي
النزاع اللفظي
شرح المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'non-existence' refers to an actual entity that possesses the property of non-existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'non-existence' is a conceptual combination (a restrictive combination) with no external referent. It is a mental construct, not a real thing.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how should the concept of non-existence (al-maʿdūm) be understood?
A) As an actual thing that exists in external reality
B) As a restrictive combination (tarkīb taqyīdī) with no external reality
C) As a simple idea without any conceptual structure
D) As a property that applies only to mental entities

Key Takeaway
The concept of non-existence is a restrictive combination, resolving the objections and showing the dispute is verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself treating your own past non-existence or future death as something that 'exists' in your mind, giving it more weight than it deserves? How would Al-Jurjani's explanation that non-existence is a mental concept, not a real thing, change the way you think about your own beginnings and end?
?
Consider a theological or philosophical disagreement you hold strongly—could it be, like the dispute in this passage, merely verbal? What would it cost you to treat it as such and let go of the need to be 'right' about it?
?
When you think of Allāh as existing eternally and everything else as contingent, do you ever imagine nonexistence as a 'place' or 'state' that things come from? How does Al-Jurjani's analysis challenge that picture, and what does that mean for your reliance on mental images versus revealed truths?
